Title:
Modelling and control of wind 
turbines for fast frequncy support
DTU Wind Energy is a department of the Technical 
University of Denmark with a unique integration of 
research, education, innovation and public/private sector 
consulting in the field of wind energy. Our activities 
develop new opportunities and technology for the 
global and Danish exploitation of wind energy. Research 
focuses on key technical-scientific fields, which are 
central for the development, innovation and use of wind 
energy and provides the basis for advanced education at 
the education.
==PAGE:3==
Modelling and Control of Wind Turbines for Fast Frequency
Support
Mechanical evaluation for the determination for use as an ancillary service
James Liam Raymond Hakim
Abstract
The current and foreseen high penetration of inverter based renewables into the power systems imposes the
requirement for advanced solutions to maintain and sustain the frequency stability in the network. With the
transition to renewables, a reduction of conventional generation and hence power system inertia is experienced
and wind turbine generators may have the opportunity to emulate the behaviour of synchronous generators
during frequency disturbances. This can be achieved by providing Fast Frequency Response (FFR), which is a
compliment to the FCR energy market and involves an almost instantaneous active power injection to combat
loss of system frequency. This thesis explores this possibility through simulations with HAWC2 aeroelastic
software. This software is employed on the 15 MW IEA reference turbine to provide state of the art answers
regarding the mechanical stresses and turbine responses under diﬀerent scenarios. This turbine has a modiﬁed
controller which enables FFR at any given time, and is designed around the ENTSO-E’s Nordic FFR market
pre-qualiﬁcation regulations. The simulations aim to investigate and answer the research questions regarding:
How a turbine can be controlled to meet the Nordic regulations? How much overproduction is possible? And
ﬁnally how this FFR response from wind turbines can be implemented into the grid?
The thesis simulates varying magnitudes of over productions across a range of wind conditions to build
a reference and understanding of what the turbines capabilities are. This is used to inform the wind farm
operators (Balancing Service Provider) of their ability to bid on the FFR market, and is conducted in three
stages. The ﬁrst parametric study explores the maximum amount of kinetic energy that can be extracted
from the rotation in the rotor without exceeding mechanical or electrical limitations. This is completed for
power increases between 4 and 11% of the turbines rated capacity. The second parametric study investigates
the response under all ENTSO-E conditions and investigates the loading on vulnerable components including
the tower base and blades and shaft bearing; this is conducted for power increases between 0.5 and 9% of
rated power. Finally, an optimisation of the second parametric study through employment of blade pitch
actuation is brieﬂy investigated for a 5and 9% increase in power. All studies are completed over a wind speed
range of 3 to 10 m/s, relating to the cut-in and rated wind speed of the machine.
The results show that under steady wind conditions, the turbine can successfully provide FFR support
under the Nordic regulations, and can employ both torque and pitch control to optimise the response time and
the magnitude of injection. The loading response from the thrust, blade and tower root bending moments as
well as the shaft bearing bending moments are analysed extensively. The results generally showed that when
compared to regular turbine operation, the overall maximum baseline loads that the machine is designed to
withstand are not exceeded. The application of this FFR support is then examined on a wind farm scale,
where the coupling of turbines to provide a more robust FFR support is explored.
The conclusions of this study indicate that FFR is a promising new revenue stream for wind farm managers
operating as Balancing Service Providers. The results under simpliﬁed conditions show that current large
oﬀshore wind farms can employ their full capacity to support the FFR market with little mechanical stress
and therefore little loss of service life.
Furthermore turbines oﬀer the unique property of a high rate of
dispatch which can fulﬁl requirements from Nordic TSO’s.
Danmarks Tekniske Universitet

Acknowledgements
Studying abroad in Copenhagen as a master’s student was a magniﬁcent as well as extremely challenging
experience. In the two and a half years duration of my studies I’ve encountered countless people who have
supported me in these trying times ranging from new colleges and our many sleepless nights in the DTU
library, to all my friends around the world who’ve supported me from afar. All these people are directly and
indirectly responsible for shaping my academic career and the proud person I have now become. Here is a
small tribute to all the people and entities who made this success possible.
Foremost, I wish to thank my supervisors Anthanasios Barlas, Anca Hansen, and Kaushik Das at DTU
Risø. They had given me the opportunity to work on this cutting edge topic to help shape a more stable
future energy grid, and facilitate the urgent transition to renewables. Following the amazing work already
done in the ﬁeld by these experts, it was a great privilege to help contribute to it and am very glad to have
had a part in it. It was only because to their careful guidance, enthusiasm, help and perseverance that I was
able to complete the thesis and the MSc in Sustainable Wind Energy.
Also importantly, I would like to thank my friends and family back home in Australia who, as well as I,
had to suﬀer the long separation as a result of the recent pandemic. Their love and motivation has kept me
pushing through the toughest times and have had them in my heart during my endeavours.
I am grateful for the amazing preparatory undergraduate education that the University of Melbourne, and
the University of Malta had given to me. My resulting Bachelor in Mechanical Engineering had reinforced,
clariﬁed and enhanced me in the works I conducted during my academic and working life here in Denmark.
The Superior education I received was extremely beneﬁcial and I would personally like to thank Professor
Rackel San Nicolas who made my transition to DTU a reality and supported me every step of the way.
Finally, a special recognition to the institutions in Europe who have supported and enriched my life with
their extreme high standards and excellence in sustainable energy education.
My home university DTU
and my Erasmus University the University of Padova have both superseded my expectations of what I had
believed to receive from them, and I am eternally grateful for them having had accepted me as a student and
am proud to have graduated from such multicultural and multitechincal institutions.
I would also like to acknowledge the paramount motivation as to why I have sacriﬁced and pursued my
extended education in sustainable energy at one of the leading institutions furthest away from home. The
world is in a critical state of desperate emergency and is often blinded in a hopeless despair. The immediate
transition to renewables, a circular economy and a greener future are of supreme importance but can often
seem unattainable. I hope this work will motivate others and help in the ﬁght against climate change and
I would like to acknowledge everyone around the globe who have directly and indirectly contributed to this
cause. There is still hope.
”They say that sometimes we go around feeling pity for ourselves,
but behind our back a great wind is carrying us”
Tony Soprano

Modelling & Control of Wind Turbines for Fast Frequency Support
Danmarks Tekniske Universitet
1
Introduction
Renewable energy generators are currently playing a crucial role in the ﬁght against climate change by
drastically reducing global greenhouse emissions through the avoidance of carbon positive fuels such as oil,
gas and coal to produce energy. This has resulted in investigations into the stability and security of supply
for today’s and tomorrows electricity grid. Wind penetration into the energy market is intended to increase
drastically to fulﬁll governments climate goals, where by 2050 50% of Europe’s electricity supply is expected
to come directly from wind. While this is a positive action to reach the predeﬁned green targets, it may come
with its own negative eﬀects.
Consistent and safe power supply is crucial for any political entity, and to ensure the transition and accep-
tance toward non-dispatchable renewable’s including wind and solar generation, the future energy security
must be addressed proactively. The security of supply and robustness of the grid must be enhanced through
regulatory measures which can accommodate events of system frequency loss (either due to the tripping of
a large producing unit or HVDC connection, or the sudden increase in energy demand from the grid). This
is crucial because with the large penetration of renewables, many will have to employ power electronics at
a farm or generator level. Furthermore, along with more national energy trading (imports and exports via
HVDC lines) to balance the grid, both will contribute to systems with low inertia’s [1]. The power system
inertia is the ability for a grid to oppose changes in system frequency due to the resistance provided by the
rotating masses often found in conventional power stations.
Renewable power generating units often have an interface between the converter and the grid which de-
couples the frequency of the grid to the renewable energy generator. This therefore results in no addition of
generator inertia being fed into the system, which in turn lowers the total system inertia. This recent issue
has given birth to the new energy market referred to in the Nordics as the Fast Frequency Reserve or Fast
Frequency Response (FFR) market, and was tested in the summer of 2020 as the Nordic Inertia challenge
in the Nordic Synchronous Area [2]. Luckily, Wind Turbine Generators (WTG’s) oﬀer a unique property in
which a fast frequency response could be implemented to quickly correct for any sudden frequency deviations,
and could potentially therefore compete in this market and help re-stabilise the grid.
The FFR market does not replace the current Frequency Containment Reserve for Disturbances (FCR-D
markets), but instead compliments it. The FFR therefore takes over as the ﬁrst mitigation measure in situa-
tions where an energy system with low inertia characteristics experiences a large reference frequency incident.
These large reference incidents result in large and sudden imbalances in the frequency and can be caused
through the tripping of a large production unit or the disconnection of HVDC links which are connected to
other synchronous systems. This ancillary services is a clear example of how grid code requirements must be
anticipated to change globally in order for each synchronous zone to increase their respective energy security.
Currently participating in this market are alternative technologies with the capability of a fast release of
kinetic energy and includes but is not limited to the use of batteries, super capacitors, and other reserves
with a high dispatch rate. Inertia can also be physically created through the use of ﬂywheels, or through
the continued use of conventional generating units or biomass/gas Combined Heat and Power (CHP) plants
within the energy mix. Finally, load shedding can also be employed to maintain balance in the grid but often
at a high cost and should be avoided as a single strategy. Hence, this thesis aims to address the problem of
whether or not it is appropriate to invest in these alternative ancillary services, or if Wind Turbine Gener-
ator’s (WTG)’s can sustain over production pulses as requested throughout their service lifetime to combat
this loss of inertia through injections of active power.
1.1
Motivation and problem formulation
Power systems consist of a multitude of synchronous machines operating in unison, where this operation
occurs at the nominal 50 Hz in Europe and most of the world. Modern WTG’s also employ synchronous
machines to transform the mechanical energy contained in the wind into electrical energy utilised by the grid.
The swing equation shown in equation 1.1 can be used to display the dynamic behaviour of a single WTG i,
with respect to the balance between the mechanical energy in the wind and the demanded energy from the
generator, and its individual impact on system frequency.
Pmechanical,i −Pelectrical,i = Jiωi
dωi
dt
(1.1)
The diﬀerence in the Pmechanical and Pelectrical for unit i gives a net acceleration in rotation as a result of
a net torque generated to correct for this diﬀerence. Where Ji (kg.m2) is the combined physical moment
of inertia for the given generator and its turbine, ωi (rad/s) is the mechanical rotational speed and dωi/dt
(rad/s2) is the mechanical rotational acceleration for generator i. This can also be expressed in terms of the
1

inertia constant H on a per unit base, where H is given in equation 1.2 as shown below [3]:
Hi = 0.5
Jiω2
nominal,i
Pnominal,i
(1.2)
Where ωnominal,i is mechanical rotation associated with the nominal frequency of the system, and Pnominal
is the nominal power of the generator. H is a non dimensional constant and is often used to compare the
inertia of the machine with others contributing to the system. Equation 1.1 can be expressed in terms of the
inertia constant and power on a per unit base as shown in equation 1.3 for Generating unit i below.
∆Pi = Pmechanical,i −Pelectrical,i = 2Hi
dωi
dt ωi
(1.3)
The dominating dynamics for a whole power systems frequency comprises primarily of the aggregation of the
dynamics of each synchronous generators each given by i; therefore dynamics of the system can be expressed
as the dynamics of a lumped machine, which has inertia constant Hsystem. This is conducted through the
weighted sum of inertia constants given in equation 1.2 for each collective individual machine i. This results
in a ﬁnal dynamic relationship for the system given by equation 1.4 [3]. Where, f is the dynamic frequency
of the system which must be maintained at the nominal frequency given by fnominal, and df/dt deﬁnes the
Rate of Change of Frequency (RoCoF) of the system.
df
dt = ∆Pfnominal
2Hsystem
(1.4)
Hence, a relationship between the inertia constant of individual generators and therefore of the whole system
to its inﬂuence on the system frequency is established. It can be seen that for systems with large grid system
inertia’s given by Hsystem, a lower df/dt or RoCof is achieved, and any momentary imbalance between
consumed and produced power from combined generators will result in only a small change of frequency.
Therefore in a system with a loss of inertia and hence a loss of inertial response, any mismatch in supplied
and demanded powers inversely will result in larger more unstable deviations of frequency. Traditionally,
conventional power plants have a high inertia (high Hi) contributing to a high system inertia and therefore
naturally achieved a stable grid. However, inverter based renewables have contribute no system inertia, hence
it is now crucial to somehow compensate this loss.
Although renewables such as wind turbines have a substantial amount of physical inertia contained in
the rotation of their rotors and generators, they are decoupled from the grid through the inverter and hence
results in a total system with a lower Hsystem. This is because most conventional wind turbines are variable
speed and operate at diﬀerent optimal frequencies of rotation with respect to the Wind Speed (WS). However,
a wind farm operator could potentially choose to take advantage of the stored physical inertia contained in
the rotor when the grid frequency drops below nominal and mitigate any instability eﬀects in the system.
WTG could therefore bid in the FFR market to supply and bring stability to the grid by utilising this inertia
through an instant release of its contained kinetic energy as an active power injection.
The FFR market is extremely beneﬁcial for the TSO’s (Transmission System Operator), who act as
managers of grid stability and safety, but also potentially for the wind turbine operators too. The market
will allow for an extra revenue stream and hence for more strategic use of wind farms providing power
to the energy grid, while also providing the crucial power required by the TSO’s at times of frequency
deprivation. Furthermore, this property of wind turbines also increases the acceptance and likelihood of
large scale approvals from Government and will inﬂuence new policy change which will drive a faster energy
transition and ensure a safer stronger grid. However, this ancillary service may also come at a cost as previous
studies have shown mechanical stresses to increase during the overproduction strategy [4]. Henceforth the
primary motivation of this thesis aims to investigate the possibility of qualifying for the FFR market, and
assessing the mechanical loads to determine if the turbines are capable of providing this ancillary service.
HAWC2 aero-servo-elastic simulation software is employed on the 15 MW IEA-RWT (International Energy
Agency - Reference Wind Turbine) to provide the most realistic up to date answers under diﬀerent loading
scenarios.
The motivation for the use of the 15 MW RWT is to conduct the most accurate model for future wind
parks through simulations of only the most appropriate wind turbine possible. Hence, for this thesis, an
important emphasis is placed on oﬀshore wind turbines and parks. This is due to the larger scale capability
that oﬀshore farms oﬀer when compared to their onshore counterpart and the foreseen expansion of the
oﬀshore wind sector. Hence, it should be expected that oﬀshore wind farms will play a more crucial role
in over supplying power given a higher power potential per wind turbine and higher individual farm size.
Furthermore, this is reinforced by analysing the projections of the development of oﬀshore wind globally.
2
==PAGE:13==
Modelling & Control of Wind Turbines for Fast Frequency Support
Danmarks Tekniske Universitet
Where estimates show huge growth in both the scale of each oﬀshore farm and individual oﬀshore WTG’s.
Chief Executive Oﬃcer of wind Europe recently said ”Oﬀshore wind is no longer just about the north sea,
its rapidly becoming a pan-European aﬀair. Poland, Spain, Greece, Ireland, the three Baltic states all have
plans, and the rapid advance of ﬂoating will help the build-out in the Atlantic, Mediterranean and black sea.”
Furthermore, the EU Oﬀshore Renewable Energy Strategy (ORES) mapped out the regulatory framework
for the expansion of oﬀshore wind development, and has set a target of 300 GW of oﬀshore wind for the
EU by 2050 [5]. Finally, mapping and analysis from the 2021 ESMAP World Bank report estimates there is
over 16,000 GW of oﬀshore wind technical potential resource in developing countries alone [6]. Hence, when
considering the capacity of the individual turbine to represent the near future WTG, the 15 MW IEA-RWT
is chosen as it is expected that oﬀshore wind will have more of an impact in the FFR market.
1.2
Objectives
The primary objective of the thesis is to use mathematical modelling tools to answer some research
questions derived from the motivation and problem formulation section above. This will help to understand
the ability of the WTG’s to supply the reference Nordic FFR market as an example of other FFR markets
that are expected to arise in the coming future. These research questions are clearly stipulated below:
• How can the turbine be controlled in order to meet speciﬁc regulations of a fast frequency market, such
as those speciﬁed by the Nordic Challenge to supply their FFR market?
• How much overproduction is possible, and how can this support be supplied?
• How can WTG’s be optimised through better control and farm coordination?
The thesis will attempt to answer these questions by modelling the 15 MW IEA-RWT as this is the new
benchmark for future turbines. This turbine gives a more realistic data set as more oﬀshore wind is expected
to drive the wind power share in the market signiﬁcantly. The 15 MW IEA-RWT will have capacities and
rotor sizes comparable to existing and future oﬀshore WTG, where they will have a custom made controller
built speciﬁcally for this analysis to simulate the TSO requirements speciﬁed in the ENTSO-E report [2].
Using these speciﬁcations, this basic controller will act in time domain simulations using HAWC2, where the
loading is recorded and analysed. The results of these simulations will determine the capability of the turbine
to overproduce in diﬀerent ways, and will allow to observe the associated loads under each scenario. This will
help answer the research questions, and hence inform whether or not wind turbines are capable of partaking
in the FFR market; and if its feasible for the WTG operators to subject their machines to this extra loading.
Parametric studies (sensitivity analyses) are conducted to explore whether a long, short, or zero support
duration is possible under diﬀerent speciﬁed conditions. This is conducted over a variety of constant WS’s
within the IEA-RWT’s cut in and rated WS following the conclusions made in the chapter 2 state-of-the-art.
These varying wind conditions will also be exposed to diﬀerent magnitudes and duration’s of overproduction
support. The ﬁnal goal is to develop a series of results which could be used by Wind Farm (WF) operators
to help determine what is possible given forecasted weather conditions, and hence make a more informed
bidding strategy for the FFR market.
1.3
Assumptions and limitations
The results of this thesis are based upon the ﬁndings and outputs from HAWC2’s Aeroelastic software.
Although reasonable loading calculations are assumed, the important assumptions are explained here to aid
in the further development required in this area.
The largest assumption is the choice in turbine to explore the research questions. Each model turbine will
have a diﬀerent capacity, structure, and blade moment of inertia which will result in diﬀerent responses per
loading scenario, and diﬀerent mechanical and electrical limitations which can be imposed on the machine.
The IEA 15 MW reference wind turbine is chosen as it is a suitable representation for any near future can-
didate oﬀshore wind turbine. The 15 MW capacity magnitude was decided through a collaboration between
NREL and DTU so as to not be too aggressive with expected technological innovations for the coming years.
However, new turbines today have already achieved or surpassed a 15 MW rated capacity; therefore might
indicate that this assumption was too conservative of a prediction and is more realistic for modern day oﬀ-
shore turbines. These turbines include the Haliade-X and Siemens SG 14-222 DD 14 MW turbines (where
Siemens claims it can operate at 15MW in ’Power Boost Function’ mode), and MingYangs 16 MW oﬀshore
turbine. More accurate modelling for future oﬀshore WF contributing in an FFR market should include a
larger turbine simulated for more appropriate results.
Technical assumptions are primarily conﬁned to the HAWC2 model which naturally has a ﬁnite level of
3
==PAGE:14==
Modelling & Control of Wind Turbines for Fast Frequency Support
Danmarks Tekniske Universitet
ﬁdelity. The model assumes many things and contains many limitations with respect to real life operation
comparisons. For example a power law wind proﬁle is selected instead of a logarithmic curve, where the
exponent coeﬃcient is assumed to be 0.2 as suggested by the IEC guideline [7], however this realistically
varies depending on location of WF and may aﬀect overproduction performance. More speciﬁc assumptions
include a steady wind being applied to all simulations in the interest of simpliﬁcation during the iterative
process of building the new controller.
Furthermore wind shear, tower shadow and turbulence were also not included for same such reasons.
This will aﬀect the loading response of the turbine signiﬁcantly as higher extreme loads are experienced when
accounting for wind shear and tower shadow due to the introduction of a 3P frequency. An increase in fatigue
loads also occurs when accounting for turbulence due to the ﬂuctuations in the wind and the resonant re-
sponses from the structure. The ability of the controller built in this study is unable to cope with turbulence
inputs adequately and should be explored in future works to incorporate feedback control which could deal
with frequency oscillations.
The 15 MW RWT is designed as an oﬀshore turbine, however this study excludes the monopile, transition
piece, and the accompanying hydrodynamic and soil responses from the model. Therefore, the turbine is
simulated as a ﬁxed bottom onshore turbine instead. It is assumed that this will have no signiﬁcant impact
on the machines ability to overproduce and supply FFR. The baseline study simulations are also conducted
under all these simpliﬁed conditions for more appropriate comparisons. Henceforth the response of the un-
modiﬁed turbine also neglects these phenomena and therefore the results and setpoints found. This setpoints
are used in the FFR controllers and therefore also do not account for this natural property. Re-calibration of
the FFR controllers variables and setpoints would be essential if future works are to implement these wind
conditions. Hence it can be seen that many details are neglected primarily due to simpliﬁcations of the model
and therefore the results should be viewed with some level of caution as the ﬁdelity is always limited.
The required FFR injection regulations are deﬁned by the duration’s of activation, support, buﬀer and
recovery times which deﬁne the overproduction ’shape’. These could all potentially diﬀer with respect to
local/national grid codes and their speciﬁc requirements. It is assumed for this study that any future fast
frequency response speciﬁcation will similarly follow those deﬁned in the Nordic Challenge Article [2]. There-
fore, it is assumed that related requirements which would impose similar electrical and mechanical loads and
responses would be suggested from other TSO’s globally for their own respective FFR market.
The assumptions related to the design and implementation of the controller must also be discussed. The
controller created is designed around the Nordic requirements but is relatively basic when compared to the
Proportional-Integral-Derivative (PID) closed loop feedback turbine controller (basic DTU controller). This
is again a result of simpliﬁcation of the initially complex problem in order to obtain acceptable results. These
results are assumed accurate enough to safely answer the research questions posed in this thesis and guide
future work in this area. Hence, a PID feedback controller is not implemented, where only a feedback loop
is used to create the desired outputs at speciﬁed time intervals. However, it is assumed that the results from
this FFR controller are suﬃcient enough to prove the capability of the turbine to supply this ancillary service.
When simulating this controller in the ﬁrst and second parametric studies, no minimum rotor speed is
assumed. The rotor is expected to decrease its energy and is allowed to decelerate below the minimum given
value of 5 RPM. This lower RPM limit is used by the basic DTU controller to avoid the crossing of natural
resonance frequencies of the structure. However it is assumed that as long as recovery is made possible
by maintaining a higher mechanical torque than the electrical demanded torque (while avoiding excessive
excitation), then the brieﬂy lowered rotor speed is acceptable for this study.
The second parametric study simulates the turbine under the full conditions stipulated by the regulations
deﬁned in the Nordic inertia challenge [2]. The article states that a long or short duration is possible, and
based on results in the ﬁrst parametric study, the short duration supply is used. Therefore all simulations
following the second parametric study have a support duration of exactly 5 seconds and is conducted with
an activation time of 1 second. The choice of 1 second instead of the two other proposed options of 0.7 and
1.3 seconds, is based on simply exploring the mean value between the activation frequencies. However, it
is assumed that an activation time of less than 1 second is easily applicable and even more appropriate for
future works.
Finally, the implementation of pitch actuation during FFR is primary based upon the assumption that
turbines operate at a suboptimal region for a short window below rated wind speed. This is to avoid operating
at a rotational speed (5 RPM) which crosses a structural resonant frequency, and hence gives the opportunity
to brieﬂy increase power by pitching to the more optimal level while the FFR cycle is occurring. It is assumed
in this study that all WTG’s operate in this way, hence this methodology should only be implemented if
applicable.

==PAGE:15==
Modelling & Control of Wind Turbines for Fast Frequency Support
Danmarks Tekniske Universitet
1.4
Thesis structure
The presented thesis is divided into the following sections where each is explained in detail in the following
sub-chapters.
1.4.1
Chapter 1: Introduction
A general introduction to the project is presented, focusing on a description of the problems involved in
providing a fast frequency response from wind turbines. The objectives to be accomplished from the thesis
are then carefully explained, where the research questions are presented clearly. Finally, the motivation for
supplying this market is discussed followed by some of the limitations and assumptions of the simulated
model and the concurrent results.
1.4.2
Chapter 2: State-of-the-art
This chapter provides an analysis of the in-depth research concerning the loading experienced on the
WTG, the control strategies used to allow eﬀective and stable functioning of the turbine. Works which are
closely related to the thesis are also explored so as to ensure this study compliments old work and does not
repeat studies. The HAWC2 aero-servo-elastic modelling software and the most up-to-date reference wind
turbine are also introduced. In addition, the aim of the chapter is to put each aspect into the context of
supplying the FFR market to then gain a complete view of the problem and the tools used to answer the
research questions.
1.4.3
Chapter 3: Model characterisation and design for Nordic speciﬁcations
This chapter explains the IEA-RWT in detail as the model will be build around this turbine. Furthermore,
the concept of a fast frequency response market is explained more deeply and focuses on the Nordic inertia
challenge as a realistic reference. Articles by the ENTSO-E [2] are explored of which it describes the controller
requirements which must be enforced on a WTG during activation of over production. In this article, the fast
frequency response market called the FFR market is considered a solution to the Nordic inertia challenge,
hence the regulations surrounding fast frequency control are explained in this chapter, and are used for the
controllers characterisation and design.
1.4.4
Chapter 4: Modelling and control
This chapter describes the speciﬁcs of the HAWC2 modelling in detail, and the implementation of the
FFR controller. The iterative process in which the controller is developed is explained, and the inner workings
are also described. This includes the ideology and control mechanism as well as its limitations, issues and
recommended future works.
1.4.5
Chapter 5: Technical results and discussion
The results from the simulations are explored and explained in detail. These results are tabulated and
graphed for two parametric studies and for the pitch control implementation study.
This chapter is the
backbone of the study and will support all reasoning and conclusions made and determines the thesis’s
objectives. The results focus on the torque and generator responses, as well as the structural loading which
occurs following the activation of the overproduction.
1.4.6
Chapter 6: Implementation of FFR on a Wind Farm level
Results from the previous chapter are used here to demonstrate how the application of the FFR should be
applied on a farm scale. The coordination between turbines for use of assistance and support is also explored
and conclusions are made about the recommended application and size of FFR provision within an oﬀshore
wind farm.
1.4.7
Chapter 7: Conclusion and future work
The main conclusions of the thesis are presented through a review of the objectives set at the beginning
of the work. Through comparison with the results obtained in chapters 5 and 6, the research questions are
directly addressed. Finally, future works related to the thesis are discussed and suggested.

==PAGE:16==
Modelling & Control of Wind Turbines for Fast Frequency Support
Danmarks Tekniske Universitet
2
State-of-the-art
This section aims to compile and stipulate all the most current and up-to-date research and tools which
will be used and implemented in this study. The diversity of resources are used to help generate a better
overarching understanding of the situation, speciﬁcally the loading on the structure during overproduction
to meet the requirements described by the Nordic inertia challenge’s FFR market. Hence an understanding
of the aerodynamic forces applied on the turbine during the regular production control strategy is described,
as well as the controllers basic functions and regions. Therefore an increased comprehension of the loading
during over production is gained, and will facilitate in understanding the results acquired in this thesis. An
introduction of the software tool HAWC2 and the chosen WTG is presented; where the HAWC2 software
is the backbone of the thesis. It is used to simulate this turbine under speciﬁed scenarios and allowed for
the iterative testing of the custom built controller. This explanation of HAWC2 is provided through a brief
understanding of the inner workings of the tool and its limitations. Finally, the chosen reference turbine is
explained to help better understand this choice of WTG, and its technical characteristics and components.
This chapter also explores and presents the most up-to-date research which was conducted leading up to
the modelling and testing of the reference wind turbine. This literature review explores the Fast Frequency
Response (FFR) reserve market, which is explained to help the reader grasp the concept of using the active
power contained in the kinetic energy of wind turbines rotor as a means to substitute lost inertia to stabilise
the grid. Hence, grid inertia is also explained as well as the issues associated with the overproduction of wind
turbines, where the relevant results from previous studies are presented chronologically, and helped shape
the thesis direction.
The aeroelastic code HAWC2 is a tool which is primarily used for calculating a wind turbines response in
the time domain. It is a code that has been developed for over a decade and is used everyday in wind turbine
manufacturing industries globally. It was ﬁrst developed in 2003 at the aeroelastic research program at Risø
in the National laboratory for sustainable energy in Denmark. The aeroelastic code contains an aerodynamic
part to determine the wind loads, and a structural part to describe the dynamic response of the given WTG’s
construction. The turbine is modelled by an assembly of bodies, which mainly consist of the tower and the
blades. Each rigid part builds the component, and are connected through springs and hinges to build the
ﬁnal structure. This enables the temporal variations of the material loads in the various components and is
accomplished by calculating the dynamic response of the entire machine while being subjected to the time-
dependent loads using the Blade Element Momentum (BEM) aerodynamic model. This provides a faster
method to simulate the model when compared to other methods such as the Finite Element Method (FEM)
for load calculations (which is computationally very heavy) [8].
The model which was been selected for the use in this thesis and which is described in the HAWC2
software is based on the virtual IEA 15-MW oﬀshore IEA-RWT. This virtual structure has been designed
as a collaboration project between the Wind Energy Department at the Technical University of Denmark,
National Renewable Energy Laboratory (NREL) and University of Maine to adhere with the International
Energy Agency’s Wind task 37 [9]. This reference wind turbine is speciﬁcally chosen as it serves as an open
benchmark for near future oﬀshore wind turbines, is publicly available and is based on the technological
growth trend developments of the Haliade-X to predict realistic future turbines. Furthermore, this turbine
model represents the most up-to-date model available, and was designed to imitate the new standard of
oﬀshore WTG’s. Hence it has it’s rated capacity of 15 MW chosen between 10 and 20 MW’s. It is a Class
1B direct drive machine and for this thesis is represented as shown in ﬁgure 2.1 below.
Figure 2.1: IEA 15 MW-RWT diagram
6
==PAGE:17==
Modelling & Control of Wind Turbines for Fast Frequency Support
Danmarks Tekniske Universitet
The Wind turbine class 1B is as deﬁned by the IEC 61400-1:2019 standard, designed to cope with an
average annual WS of 10 m/s, and a reference maximum (10 min average) WS of 57 or 50 m/s (depending
on if the site is in a tropical cyclone site or not, respectively). Furthermore, the ranking of B designates
medium turbulence intensity characteristics of approximately 0.14 [7]. These varying wind conditions are
managed through employment of controllers which explicitly describe how the turbine should operate during
production. For this thesis the 15 MW IEA-RWT is modelled with the use of DTU’s basic controller. In
general the dynamic behaviour of the rotor is tightly coupled with the controller behaviour and pitch schedule
to reach the desired shaft RPM, torque and thrust.
Controllers used in turbines are often PID controllers. These controllers can be written in terms of the
Laplace variable s (frequency domain variable) to achieve an action given by y for any given input error
signal x. This is displayed in equation 2.1 below for basic reference.
y = x(Ki
s + Kp +
Kds
1 + sτ )
(2.1)
Where Ki, Kp, and Kd are the integral, proportional, and derivative constants respectively, and τ is the time
constant to prevent the derivative term from becoming large at high frequency. The integral term Ki ensures
the mean value of the zero signal x approaches zero, otherwise y would increase or decrease indeﬁnitely
according to Kp. Depending on the region the WTG is operating in, the controller’s respective constants
(which deﬁne how aggressive the controllers actions are) will change accordingly, as will the type of action
which is given by y. For example, above rated WS, the input error signal x is the diﬀerence between the
measured rotational speed, and the demanded rated rotational speed and hence y deﬁnes the amount of pitch
angle to achieve this. While below rated WS, the error signal x is given by the rotor speed and the action y
is given by the generator torque. However, for the basic DTU controller, this implementation of the WTG
control is fundamentally much more complicated than that stated above, where for below rated wind (partial
load) the DTU basic controller is schematically displayed in appendix chapter 9.2 in ﬁgure 9.1 sourced from
an article from DTU Wind Energy [10], and implements many actions to achieve its goal.
Blade pitch control is primarily used to limit the aerodynamic power in above rated WS’s in order to keep
the turbine within its design limits, while also being applied at lower WS’s as to not exceed loads at high
TSR’s. The generator torque is primarily used to limit the transmission torque in above rated wind and to
control the rotor speed below rated in order to maximise energy capture as previously stated. The strategy
used by the DTU basic controller for this optimal Cp (Coeﬃcient of performance) tracking while below rated
WS, is based on a balance between the generator torque and the aerodynamic torques to obtain a close to
optimal TSR. The controller also acts to avoid the feedback of higher frequency dynamics through use of
a calculated torque reference at each time step based on a second order low-pass ﬁltered rotor speed. This
feedback is enforced by setting torque limits to the PID controller which open up once the rotor speed is close
to these limiting bounds. In this case, the torque reference is then given by the PID through a speed error
term between the rotational speed and the setpoint rotational speed. Because the rotor speed is bounded to
avoid structural resonance and over speeding, the power loss is minimised by performing some adjustment
of the minimum pitch. A ﬁrst order low-pass ﬁltered wind speed measured at the hub height is then used
as a parameter for varying the minimum pitch angle based on a provided look up table [10]. This results in
an overall control strategy with three regions, two of which fall below rated WS. For the 15 MW IEA-RWT,
this rated wind speed occurs at 10.59 m/s. These regions are shown below:
•
3.00 m/s - 6.98 m/s: A PID controller on the generator torque is used to regulate the turbine at
the turbine’s minimum rotor speed of 5 RPM. This controller is applied until reaching rated WS. Pitch
angles are determined using a look-up table scheduled on the ﬁltered hub-height WS until reaching
optimal pitch at approximately 6.98 m/s. The pitch is regulated to achieve the maximum power point
while not exceeding thrust values as the TSR is signiﬁcantly higher for this region. Hence, pitch and
torque are controlled together to improve the trade-oﬀbetween energy and loads.
•
6.98 m/s - 10.59 m/s: The rotor speed is regulated to operate at the turbine’s optimal TSR with
a PID controller on the generator torque and an optimal constant pitch. The controller attempts to
optimise power output following a quadratic function of rotor speed. Careful switching logic is applied
before and after this region in order to ensure smooth transition between control strategies.
• 10.59 m/s - 25.00 m/s: In above-rated WS’s, the rotor speed is regulated via a PID controller on
the blade pitch angle while maintaining constant torque. The objective of the blade pitch controller
is to regulate the rotor speed to its rated value, 7.55 RPM. Hence, the TSR is reduced from optimal,
while the pitch increases from optimal as the WS becomes greater.
7
==PAGE:18==
Modelling & Control of Wind Turbines for Fast Frequency Support
Danmarks Tekniske Universitet
The basic DTU controller employs these regions on the simulated 15 MW IEA-RWT in HAWC2, and rep-
resents the typical operation of WTG’s in real life operation. Therefore it is clear that diﬀerent operating
regions have diﬀerent capabilities which can be expected with respect to the wind speed for any given WTG
(independent of the size or capacity rating of the machine). Hence to use WTG’s as a substitute for lost
inertia in the grid through fast frequency control, an understanding of this controller is essential.
Frequency balance and grid inertia are of paramount importance to a healthy and stable grid. Inertia in
the power system refers to the energy stored in large rotational generators and some industrial motors which
in turn gives them the tendency to remain rotating. In moments of frequency loss or gain, a high grid inertia
allows for a temporary response (typically within seconds) through the energy stored in these rotating sources.
Historically, grid frequency has been taken for granted as the abundance of inertia from conventional fossil,
nuclear and hydro-power generators has provided the world with strong energy grids which could contain the
frequencies to a manageable level in the events of minor disruptions.
For example, a generation loss incident can be seen in ﬁgure 2.2 where the disturbance occurs at 0 seconds
and the frequency decreases. This ﬁgure illustrates the initial slope of the frequency decline, where this slope
is deﬁned as the Rate of Change of Frequency (RoCoF), which is also deﬁned in equation 1.4 as the df/dt.
It should be noted that this RoCoF is more steep and deep the lower the systems inertia is, hence reaches
a critical frequency faster causing shutdowns or damage within the grid. Droop control follows the incident
event after a small time delay (rate of which depends on the system) and is controlled collectively by each
generators governor, which adjusts the power output to stabilise the frequency at a reasonable level. This
is also known as the Frequency Containment Reserve (FCR) and occurs in general within 30 seconds of the
incident event. Secondary control known as the Frequency Restoration Reserve (FRR) is then implemented
at a much slower rate in order to supplement the FCR and restore the frequency back to nominal.
Figure 2.2: Grid frequency primary and secondary response to a generation loss incident [11]
With the increasing loss of electrical inertia due to the large penetration of inverter based renewables, the
response time of the FCR has not been and will not be adequate enough now and in the future. This is
because the RoCoF will become too large and the reaction time of the FCR will not suﬃce to maintain the
nominal frequency and health of the energy grid at 50 Hz for the Nordic region. Therefore, a complimentary
response for faster reactions to supplement the FCR is required. Many studies have recently been conducted
with eﬀorts to mitigate the eﬀects of the loss of inertia as renewables evolve and immigrate into the energy
grid. Many strategies have developed where some involve the use of the rotational energy contained in the
rotor of WTG’s to be considered as a possible substitute of the lost inertia, and act as a spinning reserve as
conventional power stations do. This could reduce the eﬀects of a higher RoCoF in the event of frequency
loss and is referred to as Fast Frequency Response; where the strict deﬁnition of fast frequency response is
the controller contribution of electrical torque from a unit which responds quickly to changes in frequency in
order to counteract the eﬀect of reduced inertial response [3]. In the Nordics, this has been explored deeply
and is tried and tested as the FFR market, where measures are in place to predict and monitor the frequency
stability of their system in real time.
8
==PAGE:19==
Modelling & Control of Wind Turbines for Fast Frequency Support
Danmarks Tekniske Universitet
The local Nordic TSO’s have implemented a kinetic energy estimation in their Supervisory Control And
Data Acquisition (SCADA) and Energy management systems. It has observed that in 2017 and 2018, the
kinetic energy levels in the grid lie between 130 and 280 GW throughout the year. However, their analysis
showed that the future inertia will (with 99% probability) decrease hence increasing the need for more injection
of kinetic energy into the grid [2]. The Nordic TSO’s then aim to forecast the system inertia required for any
reference incident by using an empirical model of the frequency responses based on occurred disturbances.
This allowed them to estimate and monitor the instantaneous frequency minimum for a number of speciﬁed
disturbances in real time. This is used as a tool to estimate any unacceptably low instantaneous frequencies
where the TSO’s would need to take actions to improve the situation.
The impact of the variance of the weather data on the inertia level in the Nordics was also analysed and
market model simulations were used to analyse the impact on the inertia based on the diﬀerent weather.
This allows for the inertia levels to be converted into an hourly resolution as a Nordic FFR demand. The
study showed that the demand for FFR is concentrated around the summer, however large variance does
occur from year to year. One year had a need of more than 1000 hours of FFR, while the other had only
approximately 100 hours demand. For the years with higher demand, FFR is found to also be required during
spring and autumn. Furthermore, the FFR need is highest in the night when the consumption, and therefore
production is low [2]. This could be an issue as global wind in general tends to be much higher during the
day than during night [12], hence if WTG’s are to contribute to this FFR market, they may experience more
FFR support requests while operating at lower wind speeds.
A study called the Analysis of the short-term overproduction capability of variable speed wind turbines
[13] released in 2014, deﬁnes the inertial response of a wind turbine as the short-term active power contribution
to be temporarily released from the kinetic energy in the rotor. The article explores the Variable Speed Wind
Turbines (VSWT) ability to provide short term overproduction with respect to the mechanical and electrical
limits of the turbine across many wind speeds. Hence, the article’s objective is not to assess the impact
on a power system frequency proﬁle, but instead on the capability of the VSWT and its limiting factors
and cost associated. This capability is assessed through a set of simulations using a generic Type IV WTG
model (proposed by IEC 61400-27-1), which is including the mechanical, aerodynamic and electrical dynamics
relevant to active power control studies. Important conclusions were made regarding the overproduction of
turbines below rated WS (< 1 pu), some of which are demonstrated through the use of ﬁgure 2.3.
Figure 2.3: Below rated WS behaviour of VSWT in Per Unit (pu of rated wind speed) for an optimal pitch
angle of 0°[13]
As stated previously, the turbine operates under three diﬀerent regions within its operation window, where
the speciﬁc MPPT curve related to the WTG of interest is employed for regions falling below the rated WS (1
pu). During overproduction at WS’s below rated, it is not possible to increase the mechanical power and thus
to reduce the power imbalance by pitching the blades. This is because the WTG is already running within
its maximum possible production at optimal pitch angle. Hence, maximum mechanical power production is
deﬁned by the curves shown in left most graph in ﬁgure 2.3 and are presented with respect to the per unit
rated WS. Therefore, it is clear that any strategy proposed below 1 pu which follows the optimal MPPT curve
9
==PAGE:20==
Modelling & Control of Wind Turbines for Fast Frequency Support
Danmarks Tekniske Universitet
should not involve pitch actuation for further power production (assuming these curves are ﬁtted according
to the optimal WTG pitch).
The right most graph in ﬁgure 2.3 displays the power speed trajectory during a short term overproduction
at WS’s lower than the rated. This is shown for two diﬀerent WS cases depicted by ABCD, and A’B’C’D’E’
correlating to 0.6 and 0.93 pu WS’s respectively. These paths illustrate the diﬀerence in recovery expected for
over-production below rated. When overproduction is initiated for either case (ABCD and A’B’C’D’E’), an
instantaneous active power step occurs (A to B, and A’ to B’ for each respective case). The WTG rotor then
slows down gradually for the duration of over-production (B to C, and B’ to C’ respectively), when ﬁnally
the power is returned to the now lesser available mechanical power. This is given by D and D’ respectively,
as rotor speed has been decreased and then proceeds to recover back to MPPT point for that respective WS.
The important fact to notice is that this study concludes that for the low WS overproduction given by 0.6
pu (A to B to C to D), the recovery process can be very slow as the turbine is following the ﬂatter part of
the optimal MPPT curve (D to A), while at WS’s just below rated 0.93 pu, the recovery process is faster due
to the steeper curve (D’ to E’ to A’) as shown in the rightmost graph in ﬁgure 2.3 [13].
Therefore, a non linear relationship between the amount of active power extracted and its recovery time
with respect to the tested wind speed is expected and will therefore be dealt with using a WS sensitivity
analysis within this thesis. Furthermore, for higher WS’s closer to 1 pu, over-production power conveys
larger deviations in the rotational speed and hence in the recovery power as the WTG is operating on the
steep ramp of the MPPT curve right before its over-production. Hence for any over production amount,
a signiﬁcantly larger power deviation from the nominal will be experienced [13]. Therefore, recovery limits
closer to 1 pu will experience more of an impact in this study.
For all cases independent of the wind speed, the shaft torque is found to increase when applying an
overproduction step in the active power, but decreases to a lower value when overproduction is completed.
During the recovery period, the shaft torque is increasing back to it initial operation value. However, the
shaft torque and the generator-rotor swings increase signiﬁcantly when applying higher overproduction steps,
as the higher request results in a deeper drop in the electrical power after the overproduction. Finally, the
article found that for its simulations, shaft torque does not reach a steady state at the end of a 10 second
over production duration. This implies that for longer activation times, the WTG could reach excitation
which amounts to a potentially critical situation [13]. Therefore, careful evaluation of the shaft torque will
be considered in this thesis.
This article also evaluates the overproduction above rated WS. The turbine is found to decelerate during
an overproduction at these speeds, however this deceleration is not as signiﬁcant as the turbine now has the
possibility to decrease its pitch angle in order to reduce the power imbalance during overproduction. The
shaft torque is found not to increase as signiﬁcantly as in the 0.93 pu case as a result of the pitch involvement,
while also contrary to the < 1 pu case, the rotor speed starts accelerating already before the overproduction
is complete, again due to the pitch control activity [13]. However, there are some drawbacks to using pitch
control to extract power above rated winds. The ﬁrst drawback is that because pitch control of rotor speed is
a critical safety function, it can be compromised by introducing the competing objective of providing power
responses through pitch. The second drawback is that the response time at which power can be controlled is
relatively slow for pitch control [14]. Regardless, emphasis on below 1 pu is investigated in this thesis, while
above will be ignored due to these ﬁndings.
A study named the Eﬀects of power reserve control on wind turbine structural loading [14] released in
2015, describes three of the many strategies used to provide this active power injection to the grid. The ﬁrst
is a simple de-rating strategy, in which the maximum power output of the turbine is capped by lowering
the upper saturation limit of the torque. Hence the turbine generates the lowered desired power at rated
speed. However, active power injection is then only applicable when the wind is above a certain threshold.
The delta reserve is a strategy in which a ﬁxed amount of power is reserved with respect to the maximum
estimated power available for the given wind, and is conserved through de-rating. This de-rating is possible
by lowering and adjusting the torque controller to a sub-optimal for below rated wind speeds to a delta power
less than the estimated available power in the wind. Similarly, the third strategy proposed is the percentage
reserve, and employs a similar methodology in which a percentage of the estimated available power is reserved
through similar methods of de-rating. This has an advantage over delta, as working at lower wind speeds is
now made possible, while the delta reserve would only operate when the delta reserve size is less than the
available power in the wind [14].
It should also be noted that results from the study did indicate that all aforementioned power-reserve
strategies tend to decrease extreme loads at the cost of an increase in pitch actuation, while fatigue loads are
reduced in faster winds and increased in slower winds. Furthermore, an important point to note is that when
de-rating the WTG via operating at a suboptimal increased rotor speed, there is signiﬁcantly more kinetic
energy stored in the rotor which has the added beneﬁt of more extractable energy and therefore less recovery
10
==PAGE:21==
Modelling & Control of Wind Turbines for Fast Frequency Support
Danmarks Tekniske Universitet
time for the same amount of additional power requested as shown in equation 3.1 in the following chapter.
However, all aforementioned strategies proposed by this paper fundamentally require a de-rating of the turbine
which is economically not optimal as maximum production is not utilised, therefore an opportunity cost is
incurred. It is therefore not considered a viable control solution for future FFR strategies. Furthermore, the
reserve power for delta and percentage is dependant on the wind speed which is ever changing, hence bidding
on a fast frequency market will require more uncertainty and therefore less incentive for WF operators to
contribute to this market.
Another study called the Optimisation of short-term overproduction response of variable speed wind
turbines released in 2018 [4] rectiﬁes the problem of lost production, and proposes a more desirable control
strategy. The emphasis on this article is the optimisation of the response of the VSWT after a short term
overproduction below rated wind speed, as other studies have found that modern WTG are not capable of
providing this response following the frequency event without any control eﬀort [15]. Short term support is
considered in this study as an active power injection of less than 30 seconds by providing additional power
by operating the WTG in over-production mode. The extra power again comes from a release of the kinetic
energy contained in the spinning rotor. This method is simulated in the study in which an underproduction
(recovery period) is found to follow, and is characterised by a power drop below the pre-event power value.
Challenges were found in order to provide short-term overproduction responses with respect to the following
under production recovery period, hence the optimisation was conducted.
The objective function of this optimisation is to maximise the released energy during the overproduction
with consideration of the mechanical and electrical limitations and the frequency dynamics of a power system
using the Genetic Algorithm (GA) which employs three approaches. Where approach 1 assumes that the
amount of injected active power is set and is optimised against the duration of this injection. Approach
2 optimises both the amount of injected active power and the duration of each. While ﬁnally approach 3
employs the frequency derivation and RoCoF as inputs to the model. The model used is a heavily simpliﬁed
generic model which does not account for the aeroelastic response of a typical WTG under loading. The
results for approach 1 are given in the ﬁgure 2.4 shown below.
Figure 2.4: Genetic optimisation through variation of activation time for each set amount of active power
injection [4]
Results from approach 1 clearly indicate that under all constraints deﬁned in the model, 7 m/s yields the
highest amount of energy extraction for the longest activation duration. It is also worth mentioning that
a counter intuitive pattern is observed, such that as the WS increases close to rated as seen at 10 m/s, it
results in the lowest optimum power. This is because it is suddenly operating on the steep incline section of
the MPPT curve shown in the rightmost graph of ﬁgure 2.3. Approach 2 has results consistent with those
seen in approach 1, where a slightly increased maximum energy is found for the same wind speed of 7 m/s.
Furthermore, another important discovery made was that the maximised energy is obtained for duration’s
less than 5 seconds. Finally, approach 3 showed similar results to that of approaches 1 and 2. Hence the
conclusion is that the maximum energy during a short-term overproduction of a WTG can be extracted at
the wind speed of 7 m/s. Therefore, the medium wind speeds are optimal for the short-term overproduction
independent of the size of the WTG [4].
11
==PAGE:22==
Modelling & Control of Wind Turbines for Fast Frequency Support
Danmarks Tekniske Universitet
This study also simulates in HAWC2 for the results found using the GA [4]. Diﬀerent overproduction
powers (% of estimated available power) for varying duration’s are tested for the constant WS of 7 m/s as
this was determined to be the most optimal.
Figure 2.5: HAWC2 ultimate load results for 7 m/s generic WTG (5 MW NREL [16]) [4]
Results shown above in ﬁgure 2.5 illustrate that considerable increases in ultimate load are experienced over
the baseline loading for the tower base, yaw bearing, and main bearing bending moments particularly in the
side-to-side directions. The loading results also indicate that the active ramp down rate should be limited as
to not increase the stress at both the tower base and tower yaw.
A ﬁnal study called the Inﬂuence of the frequency support provided by a grid-forming control-based
wind turbine on the loading of its mechanical system [17] was released in November 2020, and investigates
the inertial response and fast frequency response supports using back-back converters. This is modelled in
Simulink using these converters to control the DC bus voltage to mimic the inertial response lost in the
system. Although this inertial response strategy will not be applied in this thesis, an important observation
was made. The increased power supply from the grid side converter results in the power imbalance followed
by the activation of the DC bus voltage which rapidly increases the electromagnetic torque. This increase
in torque is the same method implemented for an activation of over production, hence results are applicable
to this study. The simulation showed that the sudden torque jump can excite the drivetrain modes and
induce torsional vibrations in the structure. Therefore it states that although the WTG will be subject to
this sudden increased loading, it will also be exposed to prolonged vibrations aﬀecting its lifetime [17].

Danmarks Tekniske Universitet
3
Model characterisation and design for Nordic speciﬁcations
This chapter will present the 15 MW IEA-RWT in which this study is based around where the general
physical properties and speciﬁcations of this WTG are discussed.
Some components are explored more
deeply and investigated to illustrate the relevant properties which are important for the simulations and
understanding of the results made in this thesis. The Nordic inertia challenge is then presented and explained
in detail; and is utilised for the design parameters of the FFR controller which will be presented in the following
chapter.
3.1
15 MW IEA-RWT
The 15 MW IEA-RWT is a horizontal axis virtual turbine and is selected for this thesis as it is the most
current reference wind turbine and is of the highest rated capacity. This indicates that the machine is also
the most relevant for the coming years, and also is the most suitable for oﬀshore WF application. The virtual
turbine is modelled as a complete structure, and is composed of the tower, nacelle and rotor. Where all the
key parameters are summarised and stipulated below in table 1 for reference.

Table 1: IEA 15MW Key Parameters
The following sub-chapters will further explain the key components which will give an important overview of
characteristics of the turbine which relate to the over-productive capabilities of the machine. It also explores
the properties which have the inﬂuence to aﬀect the results based on the chosen wind turbine, primarily the
rotor of the turbine.
3.1.1
WTG Tower
The 15 MW RWT is primarily designed as an oﬀshore wind turbine, where a complete oﬀshore wind
turbine is composed of three primary pieces including the WTG (including tower and RNA), the transition
piece (structure that houses the WTG and sits on top of the monopile) and ﬁnally the foundation (typically
a monopile). Although an oﬀshore turbine is modelled, for the simplicity of this study the HAWC2 .htc
ﬁle only describes the ﬁrst piece (the WTG). It assumes the structure is directly ﬁxed to the bottom as an
onshore turbine, hence the study does not account for the hydrodynamic loads, seabed soil response, or the
increased length as a result of the monopile and transition piece. The tower is designed as an isotropic steel
tube and has a tower mode of 0.17 Hz.
3.1.2
Nacelle and drivetrain
The Nacelle and drivetrain employs a direct drive layout with permanent-magnet, synchronous, and radial
ﬂux generator in a compact unit. This is a common trend for new turbine models as the exclusion of a gear
box signiﬁcantly reduces the maintenance downtime and operating costs of the turbine and increases the
simplicity of construction. This assembly requires less moving parts, and therefore lower complexity and
higher reliability. This is especially important as this turbine will be employed for over production, which
will have adverse eﬀects on the drivetrain due to higher mechanical loads. Hence, with less moving parts and
13
==PAGE:24==
Modelling & Control of Wind Turbines for Fast Frequency Support
Danmarks Tekniske Universitet
components, this is seen as a positive.
The main drive shaft has a hollow cylindrical cross section with a constant wall thickness. Along with the
rotor, the shaft is supported by two main bearings within the nacelle. Both these bearings have a rotating
outer raceway and a ﬁxed inner raceway. The entire weight of the turbine rotor, generator motor and hub
loads are transmitted by the main shaft to the turret (nose) via the bearings, where the upwind bearing is
assumed to carry all axial loads and moments, while both upwind and downwind bearings share the radial
forces. Hence, the upwind bearing must speciﬁcally be analysed during overproduction as it carries many of
the expected increased loads.
3.1.3
Rotor
The thesis primarily explores the use of over production to supply the grid with supplementary power
and does this through the use of the physical inertia contained in the rotation of the rotor. This inertia
comes directly from the extraction of the rotational kinetic energy, hence any available energy in a WTG
is extracted from the rotating mass at the cost of angular velocity. The inertia contained in the rotating
mass describes the tendency of the body to resist change in its rotational speed for the given applied extra
generator torque. A large composition of this inertia comes from the blades of the turbine, and in this case
the 15 MW IEA-RWT holds blades deﬁned by the DTU FFA-W3 series of airfoils. Where the inertia for a
single blade can be calculated as shown below through integration of the mass over the total length of the
blade as shown in ﬁgure 3.1.
Jblade =
Z
x2dm
Figure 3.1: Blade Inertia derivation
The total inertia (Jtotal) for these rotating blades and hub about the shaft (its central axis of rotation) is
calculated by adding the inertia of the three blades, and hub as shown below:
Jtotal = Jhub + 3Jblade
Where the individual inertia of the hub and those of the three blades are calculated via the parallel axis
theorem, as the blade inertia’s (Jblade)’s must be corrected due to their positions relative to the main rotor
center line. The integration of the distributed mass of the rotor gives the total rigid rotor inertia (Jtotal),
and according to the technical report, is approximately 3.524605 × 108 kg.m2 [9]. This is calculated from the
blade structural ﬁle which lists the mass distribution, and hence allows the integration over the length while
ignoring coning and prebend. It is important to note that the blade inertia has a signiﬁcant eﬀect on energy
extraction and an understanding of this property is crucial. The inertia constant H for the IEA-RWT relates
to the physical inertia J as shown in equation 1.2 previously, and therefore to the radius of the blades and
its mass distribution. Henceforth, a further blade mass distribution or longer blade will result in a higher
inertia, and will therefore eﬀect the results as the inertia deﬁnes the kinetic energy stored and therefore the
amount of energy which can be extracted. This is clearly shown in the rotational kinetic energy formula
below, where J inﬂuences kinetic energy K proportionally.
K = 1
2J ω2
(3.1)
14
==PAGE:25==

Danmarks Tekniske Universitet
Hence the WTG rotor (primarily the blades) used in this thesis is of paramount importance for the amount
of energy that can be extracted (along with its rotation speed), and therefore directly inﬂuences the results
produced in the thesis. Each blade has its key properties summarised in table 2.

The blade’s design point is derived through the blade design process as a trade oﬀbetween power production

TSR is 9, while the blade pitch is 0°.
Figure 3.2: Design point for IEA 15 MW-RWT, sourced from the IEA Wind TCP Task 37: Technical report
[9]
It is important to note from the ﬁgure above that an optimal maximum operating point can operate for
any combination of blade pitch and TSR that lies within the power coeﬃcient (Cp) region of 0.48 at the
expense of diﬀerent thrust coeﬃcients (Ct). However, the original controller used in this thesis is the DTU
basic controller and is speciﬁed in the State-of-the-art section (chapter 2). This controller acts to operate
the WTG such that the minimum rotor speed is 5 RPM, and hence doesn’t allow to not intersect its 3P
frequency with the natural frequency of the tower which was stipulated in sub-chapter 3.1.1. Furthermore,
the controller allows to reach a maximum tip speed of 95 m/s corresponding to rated rotational speed of 7.55
RPM at 10.59 m/s and pitch of 0°. However it should be noted that for the lower wind speeds below rated,
the pitch is set slightly more positive as a result of the higher than optimal design Tip Speed Ratio (TSR)’s
to maintain the 5 RPM, and hence the operating point seen in ﬁgure 3.2 is not accomplished.
3.2
Introduction to the Nordic inertia challenge
Energy markets are the commodity exchange platform that deal speciﬁcally with the trade and supply
of energy. The global energy markets are managed locally by the Transmission System Operators (TSO’s)
whom accommodate and manage the supply of energy while adhering to the local energy rules, policies and
regulations of the given market. In Europe, the European Network of Transmission System Operators for
Electricity (ENTSO-E) comprises of 39 members from 35 diﬀerent countries and collectively manage and
15
==PAGE:26==
Modelling & Control of Wind Turbines for Fast Frequency Support
Danmarks Tekniske Universitet
coordinate Europe’s electricity grid. They enable the energy transition via the fulﬁlment of the mandates
given to ENTSO-E based on EU legislation, and help shape future energy regulations. The Nordic members
include East Denmark, Finland, Norway and Sweden where this region is analysed in this thesis to help shape
the fast frequency response control based on their speciﬁed requirements.
It was explained in chapter 2 that with the large penetration of inverter based renewables supplying the
electricity market, a loss of electrical inertia has been experienced. This is because renewable generators such
as VSWT’s, solar, wave, tidal or even storage technologies such as batteries or hydrogen do not contribute any
direct large rotating masses to provide resistance to combat supply and demand variations. This has led and
will further lead to an unstable market in which the RoCoF is too high, leading to critical frequencies being
met faster from smaller changes in load requirements experienced in the grid. Therefore, more initiations of
load shedding or generation disconnections (black-outs) within the system will be experienced if the frequency
is not held near its nominal value to protect machinery and keep the power system operational.
The ENTSO-E evaluated two other mitigation measures to compliment rotating masses existing within
the grid to keep the future energy grid within ±1; these include an active power injection and a reduction of
the dimensioning incident. However, the reduction of the dimensioning incident (i.e. a decrease of the output
power of the largest unit or a decrease of the planned import/export on a HVDC link, etc.) scores low in
terms of cost and is today used as a plan B. The plan A mitigation measure consists of active power injections
as it is the most promising in terms of potential, eﬀectiveness, and suﬃcience [2]. This has led the ENTSO-E
and many countries to create new electricity markets to complement the primary reserve market in a faster
way to secure frequency stability almost instantly. In the Nordics this is called the Fast Frequency Reserve
(FFR) and compliments the primary reserved called the Frequency Containment Reserve for disturbances
(FCR-D) to ensure the system frequency is maintained at 50 Hz. FFR now takes over as the ﬁrst mitigation
measure at situations with low inertia and large reference incidents in the Nordic synchronous area.
This thesis will use this speciﬁc FFR market as the basis for the design of the FFR controller to be
modelled on the 15 MW RWT. Hence the regulations surrounding FFR control are explained, and are used
for the controllers characterisation and design in the following chapter. Strict requirements are proposed
in order to secure frequency stability in the Nordics at the cost of diﬃculties of pre-qualifying a suﬃcient
amount of reserves. This is because some some generating units could not pre-qualify at all or had their
reserve capacity heavily reduced. Hence, the thesis will explore if WTG’s can contribute to this speciﬁc
market assuming other emerging fast frequency markets will have relatable pre-qualifying conditions.
3.3
Nordic technical requirements for validated FFR support
In a paper describing the technical requirements of the Nordic FFR released in February 2020, it had
concluded that any situation involving the loss of frequency is much more critical than an over frequency
situation. Therefore, the FFR described is only deﬁned for under frequency situations [18]. ENTSO-E also
stipulates the four main aspects to be considered in the design and are listed below [2]:
• Activation time: The faster the activation, the smaller the frequency deviation becomes, and therefore
the system frequency response increases. The response of the FFR is static, hence the activation is
based on a logic variable which only activates when the frequency drops below a certain threshold.
• Activation frequency: Activation at smaller frequency deviations will improve the average nominal
frequency at the cost of a higher occurrence of activation, and will also increase the system frequency
response. To avoid FFR over implementation the threshold must not be set too low while still allowing
enough time to fully activate. However, the fundamental purpose is not ensure the frequency of the
system stays above 49 Hz hence this is the priority.
• Duration: A longer duration of active power injection will therefore increase the amount of energy
supplied to the grid from the FFR source.
• Deactivation: Importantly, the deactivation must be carefully managed as an abrupt deactivation after
only a short over-production duration may result in a second frequency drop. This can be mitigated
by either extending the duration, or implementing a ramp down rate for a smooth deactivation.
Pre-qualiﬁcation of the turbine ensures compliance so that WF providers have the ability to deliver support
as required by the TSO and that all technical requirements are fulﬁlled. The WTG must be tested according
to either short duration (minimum 5 seconds duration) or long duration (minimum 30 seconds duration); as
well as the chosen activation requirements including the activation level and full activation time based on
three options shown in the table 3 below.
16
==PAGE:27==
Modelling & Control of Wind Turbines for Fast Frequency Support
