Table 3: Nordic FFR activation technical requirements
In addition to other requirements speciﬁed in the ENTSO-E technical requirements [18], the turbines rated
apparent power, and inertia constant H must be speciﬁed to partake in the market. The FFR volume is given
in MW, where the pre-qualiﬁed FFR capacity is the amount of active power supplied for the support duration
applied for by the Balancing Service Provider (WF manager). An overshoot is accepted but is limited to a
maximum over delivery of 35% of the pre-qualiﬁed FFR capacity.
FFRovershoot,max = 0.35 × (Pre-qualiﬁed FFR Capacity)
[MW]
Another strict requirement is the total allowable time for the cycle to ﬁnalise, where another FFR activation
cycle must be ready within 15 minutes after the activation instant.
Hence, equation 3.2 below displays
the total regions of a full cycle which must ﬁnalise less than or equal to 15 minutes (or 900 seconds) and
corresponds with the accompanying graph 3.3 visualising the regions.
tactivation + tsupport + tdeactivation + tbuffer + trecovery = ttotal ≤900 seconds
(3.2)
Figure 3.3: ENTSO-E FFR Technical pre-qualiﬁcation speciﬁcations and requirements (not to scale)
As can be seen in ﬁgure 3.3, there is no requirement on the shape of the deactivation post support duration.
Hence, the reduction of power can occur in a step wise fashion or gradual ramp down as shown by the red
dotted lines during tdeactivation in the above ﬁgure. However, to avoid a potential second frequency drop
as a result of a fast deactivation following a short support duration (as previously speciﬁed), an additional
criteria is implemented only for short support duration’s (minimum 5 seconds). Hence for the short support
duration, the rate of deactivation is limited to maximum of 20% of the pre-qualiﬁed FFR capacity per second
as an average over any integration time of one second, with no single step larger than 20%. This is shown in
equation below:
FFRdeactivation,rate,max = 0.2 × (Pre-qualiﬁed FFR Capacity)
[MW/s]
17
==PAGE:28==
Modelling & Control of Wind Turbines for Fast Frequency Support
Danmarks Tekniske Universitet
The buﬀer period is then implemented in which it must last for at least 10 seconds before the generator is
allowed to under-produce to recover the lost rotational kinetic energy. This buﬀer magnitude must remain at
the previous production amount before activation of the FFR controller. Recovery must not start until the
buﬀer period has ended as shown in ﬁgure 3.3, where there is no requirement for the recovery shape. However,
the magnitude of recovery must not exceed an absolute value of 25% of the pre-qualiﬁed FFR capacity. This
is a lower recovery limit and is shown in the equation below:
FFRrecovery,max = 0.25 × (Pre-qualiﬁed FFR Capacity)
[MW]
Finally, the technical report deﬁnes that an aggregated resources can be considered as a single entity, and
that new resources can later be added to this entity if new contender is pre-qualiﬁed separately and then
merged. Resources that are identical to each other can be type qualiﬁed if the individual resource is below 0.1
MW of FFR capacity delivery. In type qualiﬁcation the FFR provider has to show that the resources behave
in an identical manner to each other. Hence, same model wind turbines can be type qualiﬁed to supply a
single larger FFR capacity provided by the WF. However, at maximum only 50 MW of FFR provision is
allowed to lie behind a single point of failure [18], hence, if an oﬀshore wind farm has only 1 export cable to
shore, a limit of 50 MW may apply to the FFR capacity.
3.4
Application of the Nordic challenge for the characterisation of the model
Application of the regulations and rules stipulated by the Nordic inertia challenge are henceforth used for
the modelling of the selected 15 MW IEA-RWT. The following chapter (chapter 4) employs these rules to build
the ﬁnal controllers which are simulated and tested in order to prove if this speciﬁc WTG under simpliﬁed
conditions can meet the regulations. Furthermore the results can then be used to recommend if WTG’s in
general can contribute to the market. The new controller takes advantage of the kinetic energy contained in
the rotor and exploits this property for the active power injection utilised by the FFR market at times of need.
Chapter 5 will then test various scenarios including diﬀerent support duration’s and activation/deactivation
ramp rates and recovery limits, with the aim to complete a full FFR cycle of the IEA-RWT with a generator
response similar to that shown in ﬁgure 3.3.
Furthermore, new strategies will be brieﬂy explored in the following chapter 6, in which better application
of the 15 MW IEA-RWT could be managed through coordinated support strategies which when coupled could
together superimpose to comply with ENTSO-E regulations. Hence the overall shape of two or more turbines
could result in the ﬁnal generator response shape shown in ﬁgure 3.3, as up to 50 MW worth of FFR support
can be accommodated in one oﬀshore wind farm. Hence if less generator power is required in order for the
aerodynamic torque to overcome the requested generator torque during recovery, then the lower limit can
be reduced below the 25% limit. However, this will of course have to be compensated with another set of
WTG’s to supply the diﬀerence which exceeds the -25% limit. This is illustrated in ﬁgure 3.4 in which the
primary turbine exceeds the lower recovery limit, but the supporting turbines compensate to yield a ﬁnal
collective response which adheres to the ENTSO-E regulations.
Figure 3.4: Schematic of situation in which the primary turbine is assisted in its recovery with supporting
turbines active power injections, resulting in a ﬁnal ENTSO-E regulated FFR response

==PAGE:29==
Modelling & Control of Wind Turbines for Fast Frequency Support
Danmarks Tekniske Universitet
4
Modelling and control
This study has conducted an iterative approach in attempting to design and test the 15 MW IEA-RWT
and its new FFR controller in the HAWC2 aero-servo-elastic software. Hence, this chapter will describe the
process of the design and implementation of the new controller, while also addressing the problems which were
faced during the investigation, and how they were assessed and often overcome. The produced controllers
provided in this chapter will feed the next chapter with the results produced, where they are there analysed
and evaluated to determine the answers related to the thesis’ research questions. All motivation for the design
and implementation is sourced from the strict requirements deﬁned by the Nordic region of the ENTSO-E,
which are stipulated in chapter 3.2 of this thesis. Future works are also presented in this chapter on how
further improvement could be made regarding the FFR controllers.
4.1
HAWC2 Model
The HAWC2 aeroelastic software is utilised for the modelling in order to build and test the new controller
which can supply the Nordic FFR market. Hence, a brief explanation of the HAWC2 code which describes
this structure is presented, where the primary code utilised in baseline and FFR simulations are all presented
in the appendix in chapter 9.6. The code is presented in .htc master ﬁles; where each block within the
.htc describes the components of the structure and of the aerodynamics, in order to simulate the dynamic
structural response during operation. The bodies that compose the structure are connected with constrained
equations that either allow only certain movements between the bodies or none at all (for example if a bearing
or a rigid coupling, respectively). A main body consists also of several bodies each with a node distribution,
this allows for an allocation of lumped data at these particular points within the system. This lumped data
describes the material characteristics, geometry of the component, and during simulations the displacement
and force at interval time step t. For this study the WTG is simulated as a ﬂexible structure, hence allowing
the elastic response of the turbine to be considered under loading during all simulations.
The aerodynamic component of the code is based on the BEM theory as described by Glauert [19], and is
a combination of 1-D momentum theory and blade element considerations to determine the loads. This takes
into consideration all structural vibrations, servo-controller eﬀects and even (if requested) the tower shadow
and wind shearing eﬀects. This makes this aero-servo-elastic simulation tool a cutting edge software which
will help provide the most accurate and relevant results for this thesis. However, only simplistic conditions
are simulated, where a steady incoming wind without the consideration of wind shear and tower shadow is
provided. The control of the turbine is performed through one or more .dll (Dynamic Link Library) ﬁles
which determine the behaviour of the generator and servos.
The main inputs are contained in the master .htc ﬁle which stipulates all the information which compose
together the model for the time simulation. Through use of this .htc ﬁle, all the bodies, data ﬁles (.dat
ﬁles) and dynamic link ﬁles (.dll ﬁles) are called upon in the time domain and combine to create the full
working model which is simulated. The .dat ﬁles describe the structure and incorporate things such as (but
not limited to) the aerodynamic proprieties of blades, airfoil data, coordinates for mass, densities, moments
of inertia for diﬀerent components, etc. The control data of the system are contained in the .dll ﬁles and
dynamically manage the operation of the turbine for given wind climate inputs and structural reactions. The
simulations used in this thesis are performed by calling the HAWC2 executable and the appropriate .htc, .dll
and .dat ﬁles. This is executed with a MATLAB script which automatically calls on the windows command
prompt to execute the simulations, and outputs the ASCII ﬁles for interpretation.
Outputs are also called upon as inputs during the simulations, and is heavily utilised by the controllers.
The DTU basic controller .dll requires the time, generator speed, pitch angles for all three blades and ﬁnally
the components of the wind speed at hub height [10]. All parameters of the controller are transferred to the
.dll using HAWC2 commands for the begin init routine of the type2 dll seen throughout the master .htc’s
[10].
Once the simulation is complete, two output ﬁles are generated upon successful simulation which include
a data ﬁle with the standard extension .dat, and another with the same name but extension .sel. These are
read by another custom MATLAB script which has been designed to output the results as according to its
channel which describes the response at time t. The relevant output from HAWC2 includes those shown
below in table 4, where the MATLAB script converts these to a .mat ﬁle to be analysed in MATLAB. These
channels give the respective quantity per time step for the set duration of the simulation (given the structure
doesn’t undergo a safety shut down due to excessive loading or acceleration due to excitation).

An important input parameter contained within the .htc ﬁle is the wind vector block and is extremely
crucial for the simulations conducted. This block is varied throughout the thesis depending on the nature of
the simulation; hence each loading situation has its own master .htc ﬁle which calls upon the same .dat and .dll
ﬁles which constitute the same operation and structural properties of the RWT. Each master .htc ﬁle contains
diﬀerent wind speeds, output names, and all the speciﬁc FFR conditions (including percentage increase,
setpoint powers, pitch and omegas, etc., when FFR is called upon outside of the baseline simulations). For
baseline calculations and the testing of the new custom made controller, constant wind speeds are set without
shear and tower shadow for consistency and again simplicity. This is made possible from the wind block which
allows inputs including air density, average wind speed, turbulence intensity, shear format, turbulence format,
tower shadow, etc.
4.2
Baseline evaluation
The 15 MW IEA-RWT is publicly available and was given as the regular .htc ﬁle with all supporting
data ﬁles (.dat) and dynamic ﬁles (.dll) in order to run the simulation under normal operating conditions.
This .htc ﬁle code can be found in the appendix in chapter 9.6.1 for a wind speed of 3 m/s as an example
(excluding the .dll and .dat accompanying ﬁles), where each WS simulation requires its own .htc ﬁle. These
baseline ﬁles compile the regular 15 MW IEA-RWT with the normal structural and aerodynamical data sets
for this structure while using the standard DTU we controller for each wind speed.
These .htc’s are run for each interval wind speed within the operating window of this particular turbine
under constant wind conditions. Where the wind shear and tower shadow are neglected but would otherwise
result in larger forces being measured on the turbine as well as a 3P frequency being introduced due to these
natural phenomena.
Power response results from the baseline modelling for wind speed cases less than rated can be found in
chapter 9.2 in ﬁgure 9.2. Where the curves are utilised to determine the transient for all cases which had
generally ﬁnished after 380 seconds. However, after testing the .htc ﬁles with the new controller, slightly
longer transients were experienced where the true steady state response occurs at diﬀerent times for each
WS. This was important to determine as the steady state had to be reached before testing the FFR controller
at the cost of more computational time and power. Testing of the FFR controller for all studies to follow
occur at times stated below in table 5 for each wind speed.

4.3
Controller for ﬁrst parametric study
The ﬁrst parametric study used a preliminary basic test controller to determine how to approach the
problem, while also establishing whether or not the turbine can support a sudden overproduction. A source
code was developed and complied to create the ﬁrst iteration of this FFR controller. The concept was that
at a certain designated activation point, the control strategy would shift from the DTU basic controller to
the new FFR controller, in which a sudden increase in generator torque would allow for a sustained constant
active power injection for a set amount of time. Hence, the controller needed a start time, a duration of over
production and a set-power point which is deﬁned in this thesis as t0, dt, dpower respectively. Where the
ﬁnal variable deﬁnes a setpoint power using a percentage (dpower) of the turbines rated power.
The ﬁrst iteration of this controller explored deﬁning the setpoint as a dynamic percentage of the available
power in the wind. This .dll employed a dynamic equation which used inputs at every time step to determine
the new power in the wind. This was tested and used brieﬂy for this study, however the issue with using a
percentage of the available power in the wind is that the true available power decreased as the rotor slowed,
hence produced a non steady overproduction. This was then edited in two iterative steps to only overproduce
a set requested amount irrespective of the changing available power during FFR. The ﬁnal iteration quantiﬁed
this as a dpower% of the rated turbine capacity. The intention is to use this ﬁrst parametric study to build
a reference table to gain an understanding of what a turbine can supply for a given forecasted wind speed
with respect only to mechanical and electrical limitations (this is shown in table 9).
The controller uses inputs from the HAWC2 model to enable a feedback loop and to hence control the
overproduction. The ﬁrst iteration controller estimated the available wind through a wind speed input V , to
determine the power transmitted to the generator by factoring in an eﬃciency loss (η). A constant Cp,max
21
==PAGE:32==
Modelling & Control of Wind Turbines for Fast Frequency Support
Danmarks Tekniske Universitet
value was assumed as the wind speed is below rated and hence should be operating at maximum on the
MPPT region. The equation used per time step is shown below in equation 4.1, however was not employed
in the ﬁnal ﬁrst parametric FFR controller as previously explained.
Pavailable = 1
2ρV 3Cp,maxπR2η
(4.1)
The result of this equation per time step served to act as the estimated steady state power (Pss) for the
controllers input. However, the ﬁrst issue determined was that the estimated power in the generator over-
estimated the actual power as the Cp,max was actually higher than in real operation. This resulted in the
generator over-demanding power with respect to the requested amount as the true steady state power Pss
was actually lower. This resulted in a relatively higher overproduction amount than called upon. Hence, to
overcome this issue, the pre-determined baseline steady state values were employed in this ﬁnal controller
and all following FFR controllers to increase accuracy of the overproduction amount. Therefore the new
controller employs a constant setpoint power (Pss) determined from the baseline study as shown below in
equation 4.2. The current FFR controllers dynamic inputs include the time, torque and rotational speed of
the generator. Where these are used in the following equations shown below to ensure the output yields the
desired behaviour with respect to the steady state power (Pss) for the given WS.
Psetpoint = Pss + Prated(dpower
100
)
(4.2)
τout = Psetpoint
ωin
(4.3)
Equation 4.2 deﬁnes the new setpoint power which as deﬁned by the ENTSO-E is the pre-qualiﬁed capacity
of the providing entity. It uses the predetermined baseline steady state estimated power in the wind (Pss
determined in table 6) summed with the amount of requested power expressed as a percentage of the rated
power. Hence for the 15 MW turbine, a 5% increase signiﬁes a 0.75 MW capacity above the steady state
value. Equation 4.3 then utilises the Psetpoint to output a new torque value given by τout with respect to the
feedback ωin. This new torque is signiﬁcantly higher than that at steady state. This creates an imbalance in
the mechanical dynamics of the turbine such that the rotor slows as a result of the increased resistance, and
therefore the aerodynamic torque also decreases. Hence at each time step within HAWC2 (every hundredth
of a second), the reducing ωin is inputted and the τout is forced to increase to maintain a constant Psetpoint
following equation 4.3. The second issue observed was that an overshoot clearly occurred as shown in the
left most graph in ﬁgure 4.1. This example is sourced from an example response from a 5% step increase at
6m/s.

Power [kW]
1 second ramp power: 
5% increase at 6 m/s
Figure 4.1: Power response from step increase of 5% vs ramp up over 1 second at 6 m/s WS

Danmarks Tekniske Universitet
The overshoot seen in this example is approximately a 16% increase of the 5% demanded. Although the
ENTSO-E accounts and allows for an overshoot up to 35% of this pre-qualiﬁed capacity, an easy solution to
overcome any overshoot occurring was found. Employment of a gradual ramp up with duration deﬁned by
dtramp allows to avoid any aggressive overshoot which may later supersede 35% of the allowable limit. This
is deﬁned for the duration of dtramp preceding the activation time at t0 by controlling the torque as shown
in equation 4.4 below:
τout = Pss
ωin
+ (Psetpoint −Pss
ωin
)(time −t0 + dtramp
dtramp
)
(4.4)
Hence, by setting a 1 second ramp-up, a resulting smooth transition into the support duration is observed
every time at only a cost deﬁned by [0.5(15000/3600)(dpower/100)] kWhr. This is seen in the rightmost
graph in ﬁgure 4.1, where t0 now began 1 second earlier at 349 seconds. All the ﬁnal source code used to
compile and create the new .dll controller is shown below and includes the ramp-up implementation. The
appropriate functions are called upon through IF statements that track the time input as shown in the source
code below.

4.4
FFR controller for second parametric study
After successfully constructing the FFR controller for the ﬁrst parametric study (results of which are dis-
cussed in the following chapter 5), the additional requirements described in chapter 3.2 are applied and tested.
Following the ﬁrst parametric results which primarily indicated that recovery was possible after sustained
varying maximum production duration’s, the new requirements are added and simulated. These primarily
include a ramp down deactivation, buﬀer period, and a lower limit during the recovery period. These were
all implemented individually and iteratively tested for the response of the controller.
The results from the ﬁrst parametric study indicated that the long duration option of sustained over-
production for minimum 30 seconds is not feasible. This is because only successful implementations of 30
second duration’s were experienced for an overproduction of minimum 4% over for all WS less than rated.
However even for this overproduction amount, WS’s above 6 m/s saw a small dip in power during the support
duration due to maximum torque limits. Furthermore, it was foreseen that with the extra requirements of the
additional ENTSO-E regulated regions, producing above the minimum of 5 seconds would not be possible.
Hence, the 5 second minimum short duration option is chosen for this thesis.
Additional choices that were made include the ramp up duration options of 0.7, 1, or 1.3 seconds, as
well as the ramp down rate, and recovery approach. The 1 second activation time was chosen simply as the
medium value between the two other options shown in table 3. Option 1 would activate less frequently due
to lower frequency activation, however 0.7 seconds would put extra strain on the machine as well as potential
overshoots over the pre-qualiﬁed capacity. Option 3 would require more activation’s throughout the WTG
lifetime, and the 1.3 second activation would provide a more gentle transition into support. However, this will
also put more strain on the WTG rotor to maintain speed as an extra 0.3 seconds is used to reach support.
A comparison of activation options described in table 3 is made and is shown in ﬁgure 4.2 for the example
case of 6 m/s for a 5% increase in power.

As seen in the above responses, any activation time for this WTG is justiﬁed and is therefore completely
dependent on the choice of activation frequency whether that be 49.5, 49.6 or 49.7 Hz respectively.
No
signiﬁcant overshoot is seen for 0.7 seconds indicating that the WTG could react to activation at a much
faster rates placing WTG’s in a unique FFR market bidding position. This can be used to integrate turbines
into this market and leverage can be applied to prioritise them over other competing generators. The support
duration as stipulated previously is 5 seconds for all cases, buﬀer duration is 10 seconds to allow the most
minimal total resistance during recovery, and ﬁnally the recovery is held for a varying time until the generator
power reaches the steady state Pss. The ﬁnal parameters chosen for the second parametric study are tabulated
below as a summary.

The parametric 1 controller is used as the basis for the second, where it was updated to include the 1 second
ramp up, and the 5 second duration of over production. Furthermore, the source code then needed to be
edited to include the ramp-down and the other additional regions. This ramp-down was included in much
the same way as the ramp up as shown in equation 4.4, in which a new variable dt r was included and set
to 5 seconds. 5 seconds is used as it represents the minimum time while adhering to all conditions from the
ENTSO-E. The Nordic technical requirements states that a ramp down must occur at a rate of maximum
20% of the pre-qualiﬁed FFR capacity per second [18]. Hence 5 seconds ensures this while reaching the
recovery region as soon as possible. The ramp-up equation is utilised and changed slightly as shown below in
equation 4.5, where a ramp-down from the pre-qualiﬁed capacity (Psetpoint) to the steady state power (Pss)
is implemented over 5 seconds.
τout = Psetpoint
ωin
−(Psetpoint −Pss
ωin
)(time −t0 −dtt
dtr
)
(4.5)
As can be seen, the torque is again controlled to maintain this steady decrease in power with respect to the
24
==PAGE:35==
Modelling & Control of Wind Turbines for Fast Frequency Support
Danmarks Tekniske Universitet
rotation of the rotor (ωin) and ensures the rate of 20% [MW/s] is never exceeded. Furthermore, it should be
noted that due to this ramp down, a heavy undershoot is naturally avoided. This undershoot was signiﬁcant
which in the ﬁrst parametric controller previously exceeded the 25% lower recovery limit stipulated by the
ENTSO-E. This is shown in left most ﬁgure 4.3, where the right most response shows the deactivation and
initiation of the buﬀer period.

Figure 4.3: Power response from step decrease of 5% vs ramp down of 20%/second at 6 m/s WS. Left most
response shows natural recovery of rotor, while right shows the activation of the ramp down and buﬀer
The buﬀer region is as previously explained set to the minimum amount of 10 seconds, and is held at the
predetermined baseline steady state value determined in table 6. This is shown in equation 4.6 below:
τout = Pss
ωin
(4.6)
Testing the buﬀer region showed no diﬃculties and implementation of the recovery period was the ﬁnal step
to a fully regulated FFR cycle. The recovery period for WTG’s involves the recovery of rotor speed due to the
unbalanced generator torque exceeding the aerodynamic torque. This imbalance is further exempliﬁed due to
an increasing loss of lift force as the rotor slows, causing the blades to decelerate faster. This in turn causes
the generator torque to compensate with an increase with respect to equation 4.6, which further decreases
the rotor speed. This issue is the most critical one as setting a lower limit of 25% of the pre-qualiﬁed capacity
made the recovery and therefore whole cycle diﬃcult to complete as the rotor was already too slow at the
end of the buﬀer region. The shape of recovery is not important unless the lower limit is exceeded, or the
total FFR cycle exceeds 15 minutes. Recovery with a 25% lower limit resulted in WS cases which could
not recover, but is generally made possible with deeper lower limits (i.e. reclim > 25%). The design of the
shape of the recovery period involved meeting the lower limit instantly to facilitate recovery of rotor speed
by lowering the generator torque as much as possible. This torque is deﬁned as shown below in equation 4.7.
τout = Prec
ωin
(4.7)
Where, the Prec deﬁnes the new lower limit setpoint power level for the recovery region in which the turbine
can recover. This lower limit power level is pre-calculated within the controller as shown in equation 4.8.
Prec = Pss −(Psetpoint −Pss)reclim
100
(4.8)
Finally, the recovery region required a new strategy to deﬁne the total duration. This region only ﬁnalises
once the power level again reaches the deﬁned baseline steady state power of Pss. Hence the total duration is
25
==PAGE:36==
Modelling & Control of Wind Turbines for Fast Frequency Support
Danmarks Tekniske Universitet
dependent on the state of the rotor speed post buﬀer region, and the diﬀerence in generator and aerodynamic
torques. The power formula given in equation 4.9 is recalculated for each time step and is utilised to track
and determine whether the generator power has reached the desired level of Pss.
Power = ωin × τin
(4.9)
Like all other regions, this is achieved through IF statements, whereby this recovery is activated with a further
conditional line; ELSEIF time > (tactivation + tsupport + tdeactivation + tbuffer) THEN equation 4.7. This
line also incorporates an AND statement in which Power < Prec must also be achieved. This often led to a
divergence of generator and rotor torques which will be discussed in the results section in detail. However,
it is simply due to an excessive request of power from the machine in which it is no longer able to generate
enough from the aerodynamic forces. Henceforth it is not an issue of an inferior torque strategy, but rather
the physical limits of the WTG.
Finally, an important note is that for the cases simulated, the lower limit of 25% was exceeded with an
undershoot. This is not acceptable and can be avoided with another rapid ramp-down between the buﬀer and
recovery region, and should be explored in future works. However for this study it is neglected as the primary
issue to overcome is the ability to recover, in which another region of ramp-down (even for short duration’s)
would only put more pressure on the rotors ability to regain momentum to overcome the generator torque
demand. For future work, a ramp down should be implemented with a more robust torque strategy to avoid
an undershoot. All the ﬁnal source code used to compile and create the new .dll controller is shown below
and includes the ramp-up, support, ramp-down, buﬀer and recovery regions. The appropriate functions are
called upon through IF statements that track the time input as shown below.

4.5
FFR controller with pitch actuation
The ﬁnal iteration of controller designs includes the second parametric controller being modiﬁed to in-
corporate pitch implementation. This controller was adapted to prove that for cases in which recovery was
not possible, pitch allowed for extra power to successfully complete its full FFR cycle. This is the ﬁnal
iteration of the design of the FFR controller in this thesis, and is primarily motivated by the fact that the 15
MW IEA-RWT operates under suboptimal conditions as explained in [9]. The design point for these blades
includes a design TSR of 9, and a design blade pitch of 0°. This is slightly below the optimal TSR and pitch
of 8.5 and -1°respectively. This slightly suboptimal design point is a typical result of the average blade design
process due to a trade oﬀbetween power production and design constraining loads [9]. In other words, there
is a more optimal way to operate the turbine at the cost of extra loading, however normal operation avoids
this optimal power production to increase the lifetime of the structure (particularly the blades). Due to the
activation of the FFR controller, the WTG operates in a completely new region which therefore opens an
opportunity to exploit a diﬀerent operating condition. This could potentially occur without exceeding the
26
==PAGE:37==
Modelling & Control of Wind Turbines for Fast Frequency Support
Danmarks Tekniske Universitet
mechanical design limitations of the structure.
The design points are shown in ﬁgure 3.2 (sourced from [9]), and can be used to illustrate the idea behind
pitching during FFR control. As previously explained in chapter 3.2, the optimal coeﬃcient of power Cp is
a function of both the TSR and pitch as shown in the aforementioned ﬁgure. To maintain a high Cp, the
TSR and pitch combination must be as optimal as possible with respect to minimising the thrust coeﬃcient
Ct. For any given wind speed below rated, the TSR and pitch are held at the baseline operating point dot
shown in ﬁgure 3.2 as well as the reproduced ﬁgure 4.4 shown below. The location of this point is used as an
example to demonstrate the ideology, and may not be the exact point during operation of the IEA-RWT.
Figure 4.4: Operating point shift from baseline operation point, as TSR is reduced to supplement FFR
As can be seen above, during the activation of the overproduction, energy is extracted from the kinetic energy
contained in the rotation of the rotor and hence slows the rotor. This deceleration results in step 1 shown in
the ﬁgure, as ω decreases for the respective WS, the TSR also decreases as shown in equation 4.10 below:
TSR = ωR
V
(4.10)
Where V is the wind speed, R is the rotor radius (including the hub distance to the axis of rotation) and ω
is the rotor speed. Therefore, a direct vertical translation (shown as step 1) occurs and therefore exits the
boundary of the optimal Cp given above as 0.48 (while also reducing the Ct below the given 0.7). Therefore,
the WTG now brieﬂy dynamically operates in this suboptimal region as the rotor slows, hence through
pitching negatively as shown by the horizontal translation given by step 2, the optimal Cp can again be
achieved (or increased) while still operating on the same Ct gradient as to not exceed mechanical thrust
loading.
This is the idea behind pitching during FFR, and new inputs for pitch values are set for each region to
try and achieve the behaviour described in ﬁgure 4.4. However, because the rotor slows at diﬀerent rates
depending on both the WS and demanded increase in FFR capacity; iterative testing is used to calibrate the
required pitch parameters for diﬀerent regions to increase the Cp during FFR. This must also be achieved
while not exciting or interfering with the torque controllers ability to provide a steady increased power
injection. To introduce this capability into the existing controller, the inputs of pitch and ω is added for
utilisation in the .dll. The rotor speed is used the pitch activation variable, and initiates pitch implementation
only when the rotor speed is below its setpoint baseline value.
Before and after the FFR cycle, the IF statements now include pitch = pitch in to maintain normal
pitch operation from the DTU basic controller. However, when a new pitch is to be implemented during
overproduction, it is set by a new variable which is assigned for a speciﬁc time region. For example; the
activation region deﬁned by the IF statement bound by time > t0 - dt ramp AND time <= t0, a new
embedded IF statement is implemented speciﬁcally for the pitch activation. The embedded IF statement is
the same for each region and works on the condition that IF ω < the setpoint ωset (deﬁned per WS from the
baseline simulation deﬁned in table 6), then the normal FFR torque condition is applied while also setting a
new pitch (given as θout) as shown in the equation below:
θout = θin −θregion
27
==PAGE:38==
Modelling & Control of Wind Turbines for Fast Frequency Support
Danmarks Tekniske Universitet
Where θin is the inputted setpoint pitch, and θregion is the pitch to be subtracted from the setpoint. Hence
for each region within this controllers source code, the pitch has its own input θregion, and are deﬁned
sequentially as ’pitch ramp up’, ’pitch activation’, ’pitch ramp down’, ’pitch buﬀer’, and ’pitch recovery’.
Hence, the activation for each region is deﬁned the same way as explained above with the exception of the
recovery region, in which a new ﬁnal time variable is inputted to designate when to deactivate ’pitch recovery’.
This is accomplished with the modiﬁed IF statement which states IF omega <= setpoint omega AND time
<= t0 + dt + dt r + rec pitch. This is shown in the source code below.

5
Technical Results and discussion
This chapter will present the results from the ﬁrst and second parametric studies which were conducted
sequentially as an iterative design approach to build the controller as described in chapter 4. The two studies
are used to draw conclusions about what a WTG is capable of supplying with and without adhering to the
speciﬁcations of the Nordic TSO’s. A following subsection is also dedicated to the results of the modiﬁed
second parametric FFR controller which includes pitch actuation for an increased recovery and a more robust
power supply.
As stipulated in the previous chapter, the controller applies a desired increase in power over an available
power contained in the wind. This available power is determined from the baseline study, and is the average
steady state power achieved at each wind speed. This available power is presented in the third row of table
8. The desired increase is deﬁned as a percentage of the rated power of the 15 MW IEA-RWT turbine. For
example a 1% increase represents an active power injection of 0.15 MW over the available power. These over
production amounts are shown in the ﬁnal column of this table with respect to the % increase shown in the
ﬁrst column. All ﬁnal overproduction powers over the steady state are shown within table 8 below.

These values are used for all overproduction cases in all following parametric studies and provide the results

5.1
Parametric Study 1
The ﬁrst parametric study involves an increase in power with a 1 second ramp-up duration to avoid any
overshoot and/or any sudden impulse loading on the turbine, a maximum support duration and a recovery
with no lower limits. A MATLAB code was created and implemented as shown in the appendix to simulate
and analyse all the cases. For the ﬁrst parametric study, these cases include all interval wind speeds from 3
to 10 m/s, for all 1% interval percentage increases ranging from 4% to 11%. The initial batch of simulations
were analysed to see if the turbine had failed to recover, or if excitation occurred causing an emergency
shutdown of the machine. Each case is initially tested for a 30 second support time duration, and is allowed
to recover naturally without ENTSO-E regulations. These cases are then individually analysed to determine
if or when a failure occurs, and if it is due to excitation causing a tower ﬁltered acceleration warning resulting
in emergency shut down or due to a loss of rotor speed. Two cases are shown below where both are simulated
for a 5% increase in power for duration of 30 seconds, where the left most case is a successful recovery at 6
m/s and the rightmost case is a severe loss of rotor speed at 7 m/s (where the simulation is cut early to show
the response).

For cases which were determined to have failed (for example: ﬁgure 5.1b), their log is analysed to determine
the time of failure and cause. The log is an output from the HAWC2 simulation which yields information
at each time step of the simulation.
The exact time at which the turbine begins to become excited or
fails is recorded and is used as the new maximum support time reduced to the nearest 1 second interval
for contingency. This contingency allows to avoid approaching the resonance frequency which caused the
recorded failure. The turbine is then re-simulated for the new reduced support duration time with respect
to the mechanical limitations of the turbine, and results in ﬁgure 5.2b, where the left most case is the same
case as shown in ﬁgure 5.1b above and is used as a comparative reference.

how to avoid loss excessive of rotor speed or excitation of structure
The reason for this particular failure seen in ﬁgure 5.1b is due to a loss of lift on the blades as a direct result
of a decreasing rotor speed. As previously explained, the excessive request of energy supplied to the generator
is extracted from the kinetic energy stored in the rotation of the rotor. Hence, the over production comes at
the cost of a loss of rotor speed and when too excessive, can consequently result in the inability to recover.
This is shown in ﬁgure 5.3.

(b) Extraction of energy results in a loss of lift and aerodynamic

Figure 5.3: Torque divergence is seen and results in an unavailable exponentially increasing demand for

As can be seen above, the rotor speed decreases as a result of the excessive energy extraction as seen in ﬁgure
5.3a. Where this results in the divergence of generator and aerodynamic torques which is shown in ﬁgure
5.3b. This as previously explained induces a higher generator demand to compensate and maintain the power
limit, which counter-productively decreases the rotor speed at an even faster rate. Hence, this is clearly a
physical limit which must be managed carefully and a reduction of the support duration (hence a reduction
of extracted kinetic energy) for cases in which recovery is stressed is the solution. The results for each case
are tabulated and are shown in the appendix under chapter 9.1 in table 16.
Another limitation considered in the case by case analyses of the ﬁrst parametric results was to make sure
the upper limits of the rotor thrust and generator torques (2812.5 kN and 21.59 MNm respectively) were
never exceeded. For every case tested, the thrust was never exceeded, however the generator torque limit was
often breached. Hence the exact time at which this limit was reached for each case was recorded and used
as another ﬁlter to obtain the maximum allowable overproduction time. The same case study is used as an
example for consistency and is shown below in ﬁgure 5.4.

These values above indicate the ﬁnal maximum possible support time with respect to the mechanical and
electrical limitations of the turbine which allow recovery and avoid the breach of the generator limit. However,
it should be noted that although some cases recovered successfully and never reached the generator limit, a
dip in power is experienced. The support duration’s are set parameters in the FFR controller and should allow
for a constant overproduction during whole the support time, which is why this loss of power experienced in
some cases is interesting. The dip in power is clearly seen in the below example case for a 6% increase at 6
m/s shown in ﬁgure 5.5, where this loss of power is seen before the generator limit is reached at 27.8 seconds.
The conclusive reasoning for this generation dip seen in ﬁgure 5.5a (beginning at approximately 370 seconds)
is due to the fast rate of divergence between the RPM and generator torque seen in ﬁgure 5.5b; hence the
torque feedback is not responsive enough to produce the setpoint power as the RPM begins to decrease so
severely. Furthermore, a linear depression is also seen after this gradual decrease. This linear decline is due
to the generator torque being reached at 27.86 seconds. This is also seen in ﬁgure 5.5b, where the generator
torque becomes ﬂat at the maximum 21.59 MNm and results in this linear decrease in power as the rotor
speed reduces.

The results clearly indicate that a peak maximum energy produced is experienced at a 9% increase, were it
then slowly starts to decrease for 10 and 11% indicating a peak. The 7 m/s case shows the lowest extractable
energy, where counter intuitively the lower wind speeds seem to have the most extractable energy. This result
opposes that found in the State-of-the-art in which the article Optimisation of Short-Term Overproduction
response of VSWT [4] determined the 7 m/s WS to yield the greatest extractable energy. This could be again
due to its simpliﬁcations and assumptions regarding the operation of the turbine on the MPPT curve, while
in reality, the turbine operates diﬀerently as shown in the baseline simulation responses in ﬁgure 5.7.

TSR vs RPM
Baseline Conditions
Figure 5.7: Baseline simulation for WS less than rated showing comparative behaviour of TSR, RPM, pitch
and generator torque
The traditional model for the operation of a WTG, such as that most likely simulated in the report [4], would
assume that the turbine operates under optimal conditions following the MPPT curve for regions below rated
as shown in ﬁgure 2.3. This MPPT is achieved when the turbine operates with the optimal pitch and TSR
to maximise the WTG’s individual blades coeﬃcients of performance value Cp. However, as it can clearly
be seen above this does not occur, the turbine actually operates under 3 diﬀerent regions below rated wind
speed given by the two red dotted lines in ﬁgure 5.7.
In the ﬁrst two regions, the rotor speed is maintained at 5 RPM to avoid natural frequencies of the
structure. This is at the cost of a signiﬁcantly higher than optimal TSR and only reaches it’s optimal value
past 7 m/s. The pitch is also therefore activated for the ﬁrst region to create a suboptimal operating condition
for this TSR to be achieved. The reason the pitch must be activated is clearly illustrated in the rightmost
graph of ﬁgure 3.2, where the signiﬁcantly higher TSR will result in higher thrust coeﬃcients (Ct). Therefore
the blades must pitch to reduce the Ct value.
This essentially puts the turbine at a de-rated operating
condition for WS’s below 7 m/s, where the rotor is spinning signiﬁcantly faster than optimal. This situation
is also described in the literature review as a methodology for a turbine to reserve extra power in case of
requested FFR response. It is term the delta and percentage reserve strategy in the article called the Eﬀect
of power reserve control on wind turbine structural loading [14]. Hence, the turbine is purposefully de-rated
to avoid natural structural natural frequencies and as a result is naturally better equipped to release more
kinetic energy from the rotor at lower wind speeds.
This eﬀect would not have been modelled or considered in the study conducted in [4], and therefore this
could be the reason why higher energy levels are able to be extracted from the IEA 15 MW turbine for lower
WS’s. While at speeds closer to 7 m/s, the turbine is operating at optimal and therefore has little extra
energy to give as their is no de-rating of the WTG.
The conclusions made from the ﬁrst parametric study are that WTG’s are mechanically and electronically
capable of providing FFR, but struggle to over produce for long duration’s and/or for high percentage
increases. The highest energy yield occurs at the lower wind speeds but likely only because the turbine
is de-rated to avoid structural natural frequencies. This property could be exploited for higher bidding at
lower forecasted wind speeds, which as explained in the State-of-the-art is when the FFR demand is highest.
Furthermore, implementation of pitch actuation can be used at lower wind speeds to increase power by
pitching to optimal 0°during FFR. The energy extraction increases after the lowest WS of 7 m/s, and can be
accounted for by the increasing ω and therefore the increasing angular momentum. It can also be accounted
34
==PAGE:45==
Modelling & Control of Wind Turbines for Fast Frequency Support
Danmarks Tekniske Universitet
for by the cubic increase in power with respect to the wind as shown in equation 4.1. The 9% case gave the
highest energy yield as any further increases were leading to reductions of support time and therefore less
energy. Finally, realistic participation in the FFR market would likely involve the short duration of 5 seconds
hence the new controller is built around adhering to the speciﬁcations of this.
5.2
Parametric study 2 results
The second parametric study involved the simulation of the 15 MW IEA RWT under the strict conditions
stipulated by the Nordic inertia challenge’s technical requirements [18]. The simulations were conducted
under simpliﬁed conditions with steady wind speeds, zero presence of wind shear and neglection of the tower
shadow eﬀects. The base line study was again utilised for each of the interval wind speeds ranging from
3 to 20 m/s; and are used to make comparisons on the loading increases on the fundamentally impacted
components of the wind turbine. These components include the blades where the examined loading includes
the thrust and blade root bending moments. The tower is also considered, where the tower base root bending
moments are analysed. Finally the bending moments subjected on the shaft bearing are examined, as the
literature review indicated severe increases as a result of the over production. The baseline is also simulated
under the same simpliﬁed conditions using only a steady incoming wind with no other naturally occurring
eﬀects to better ensure more applicable comparisons. Table 11 below displays the baseline results of the
maximum value of the steady state forces and torques applied to each of respective components. Where the
tower and blade root bending moment occurs about the x axis, the shaft bearing moment occurs about the
z axis. This is consistent throughout the whole study.

5.2.1
Parametric study 2 FFR response analyses
Hence, for the second parametric study, smaller and more realistically applicable percentage increases are
included in this exploration, and include 0.5, 1, 1.5, and 2, where a 1% increasing interval follows until 9%.
Simulations showed promising results for lower increases, in which for cases 0.5 and 1% a full recovery was
achieved over all wind speeds. The case of 1.5% showed the ﬁrst failure of recovery at 7 m/s, where the
following case for 2% also had failure of 7 as well as 8 m/s. All cases following included failure of WS’s 7, 8
and 9 m/s much like the case for the 9% increase. The results from the 1% increase in active power are shown
here for 3 m/s, 7 m/s and 10 m/s, where the remaining wind speed responses can be found in the appendix
(chapter 9.2). The responses are displayed to demonstrate how the controller works to operate the generator
torque to produce the steady power response deﬁned by the ENTSO-E requirements. Also provided is the
rotor speed response and the aerodynamic torque which are crucial factors which make recovery possible.

Figure 5.11: Second parametric FFR controller used for a 1% increase at 3 m/s
The cases displayed here are chosen as they represent wind speeds within the three diﬀerent regions shown
in ﬁgure 5.7, and can be used to explain how the operational points of the turbine have an eﬀect on the
ability to supply the FFR under the strict Nordic conditions. Figure 5.9 shows the simulation of the FFR
overproduction for the lowest WS of 3 m/s. As previously mentioned, at this low WS the turbine operates
intentionally in a sub-optimal mode to avoid natural frequencies of the structure. It keeps the rotor speed
at a constant 5 RPM which results in a TSR of 20.436; this is well above the optimal of approximately 8.73.
This indicates that the RPM is rotating at a much higher speed according to TSR equation 4.10. Hence, the
higher TSR ratio results in the ω operating faster, and explains why the rotor recovers fastest at the lowest
WS of 3 m/s at only just over 40 seconds. Furthermore, the literature review had indicated from ﬁgure 2.3
that operating at higher WS’s below 1 pu will result in the signiﬁcantly larger power drop from C’ to D’
when compared to the lower at C to D. This also explains why this trend shows a better recovery for the
lower wind speed cases seen above in the 1% increase case and all following % cases.
Figure 5.10 shows the FFR operation and recovery for the 1% increase cases at a WS of 7 m/s. For
this WS, both the pitch and TSR are operating at optimal values as shown in ﬁgure 5.7 at the second red
dotted line. At this WS for the same 1% increase, the recovery time is the longest and is a direct result of
the comparison shown speciﬁcally in ﬁgure 5.10b. Here the initial diﬀerence in magnitudes of the opposing
torques between the generator and aerodynamic lift force after the buﬀer region are zero (if not negative).
37
==PAGE:48==
Modelling & Control of Wind Turbines for Fast Frequency Support
Danmarks Tekniske Universitet
If the generator torque had exceeded the available aerodynamic torque within the recovery zone, this case
would also have failed to recover because as previously explained, the diﬀerence of these torques results in
the accelerating force used to increase the rotor and therefore the lift along the blades. This is explained
brieﬂy in ﬁgure 5.12 demonstrating the equation of motion (equation: 5.1) of the Rotor Nacelle Assembly
(RNA) system.
τaerodynamic −τgenerator = J α
(5.1)
Figure 5.12: RNA system equation of motion
Where τ are the respective torques, J is the inertia of the RNA system and α is the acceleration of the system
(also given by dω/dt). Hence as can be seen from above, for there to be a positive acceleration (α > 0) during
the recovery zone, τaerodynamic must be greater than τgenerator. For 3 m/s case, it is visually very clear the
τaerodynamic,3m/s > τgenerator,3m/s for all zones including the buﬀer region; however, for 7 m/s the diﬀerence
is almost negligible at approximately 425 seconds, but slowly the divergence increases allowing the rotor to
speed up. Finally, the 10 m/s case indicates a healthy recovery rate as the τaerodynamic,10m/s > τgenerator,10m/s
for all regions after deactivation.
The negative parabolic response shape experienced in the aerodynamic torques is due to the compensation
of the positive parabolic shape of the rotor speed as both are multiplied to yield the recovery power which is
held at the -25% lower limit of the pre-qualiﬁed capacity. This response is a ﬂaw as is seen in ﬁgure 5.13.

Figure 5.13: FFR controller used for a 5% increase at 5 m/s
The ﬂaw comes from an aspect of last IF statement line of the second FFR controller design, in which it
states:
1 ELSE IF (time > t0+dt+dt_r+dt_b .AND. Power < Prec) THEN
! recovery
2
Tgen_out
= Prec/omega
3 ELSE
! normal
controller
38
==PAGE:49==
Modelling & Control of Wind Turbines for Fast Frequency Support
Danmarks Tekniske Universitet
4
Tgen_out
= Tgen_in
5 ENDIF
This line designates the operation during the recovery zone only, while the ﬁnal ELSE allows the normal
DTU basic controller to regain normal operating control of the turbine. The issue with this ELSE IF is
that for the two conditions to be met, the regular time constraint (which must be greater than the whole
support duration’s, ramps and buﬀer zones) must be achieved while also satisfying the AND statement. This
AND statement ensures that IF the available power is less than lower limit of -25% (given by Prec), then
Tgen out=Prec/omega. This power is calculated by the ωin and torquein at each given time step, and holds
the power at -25% therefore not allowing it to increase naturally. This is because as seen from results in
the ﬁrst parametric study, the power naturally drops well below -25% during recovery as seen in ﬁgure 5.5a.
However, the second parametric controller limits this and must now not be exceeded, hence this line achieves
this for however long it takes for power to be greater than the lower limit Prec.
The issue comes from the fact that by holding the power at the -25% value as seen in ﬁgure 5.13, the
lower limit is held longer than it should. The purple dotted line in ﬁgure 5.13a shows when the RPM has
recovered to back to its steady state of 5.004, however the RPM still increases as the generator torque is still
decreasing to maintain -25%. Hence the generator torque and aerodynamic power are both reduced past the
RPM ﬂag time seen now in ﬁgure 5.13b in a sort of negative feedback cycle. This results in the large overshoot
following the recovery period occurring at around 375 seconds as the rotor is over-sped and the generator
must suddenly decelerate it by again instantly increasing torque. Although this may create instability in the
grid, this overshoot following the recovery region experienced in some simulated cases does not break any
of the design speciﬁcations stipulated by ENTSO-E. However future work on this controller should involve
designing a better solution for control during the recovery period. Finally the recovery period was bound
by allowing no more than 15 minutes from the time of activation until, in which all cases that managed to
recover do so well before the 15 minute period. The following table shows the total time from activation to
full recovery for each case, where the maximum time was 259 seconds or approximately 4.3 minutes.

Table 12: Recovery time in seconds for each FFR % increase case
It can be seen that recovery times increase as the WS’s approach 7 - 8 m/s, then again start to decrease as
the energy in the wind increases. An important understanding about the recovery time is that it cannot be
improved through a better application of the generator torque as this is already as eﬃcient as possible with
respect to the ENTSO-E regulations. Recovery time is only dependent on the aerodynamic torques ability to
supersede the generator torque in the recovery period. Hence, the ﬁnal conclusions made from these results
shown in table 12 indicate that to improve these times and recover the failed cases, diﬀerent strategies outside
of torque control must be applied. Either better application of the FFR through coupling of turbines, or the
activation of pitch during FFR could result in better overall times for recovery. Furthermore, it may also
result in all cases recovering, including the 7 m/s for 1.5% and above, 8 m/s for 2% and above and ﬁnally
the 9 m/s for 3% and above, hence allowing the capability to supply the grid for all WS’s and all given %
increases. This led to the implementation of the pitch actuation investigation in 4.5, and also the coupling
of turbines explained in chapter 6.

The only case where thrust increases as a result of FFR occurs at 3 m/s as shown in ﬁgure 5.17a, where

loading’s over the whole WS window of operation to compare and evaluate each wind speed FFR case to the
maximum loads the turbine experiences.
5.2.3
Maximum baseline loading comparison
Although ﬁgures 5.15 and 5.16 show large increases in loading per wind speed, the baseline loading’s that
were presented in table 11 are utilised in this section, where the last row is the maximum loading values for a
wind speed window from cut-in to 20 m/s, where any following loads are seen to decrease or plateau. To make
loading comparisons with the case by case FFR simulations, the maximum values per case are subtracted
by the maximum values presented in the last row in table 11 to determine whether certain cases exceed the
maximum respective loading. The results are all tabulated and presented in the appendix under chapter
9.1.2.
It can be seen that almost all values presented in the tables 18 to 28 are negative which means that the
maximum values found in the last row of table 11 (for baseline loading of 11 m/s) are hardly ever exceeded
during FFR activation. This is with exception of the blade root bending moment at a WS of 10 m/s. For
each 10 m/s case, it showed an increase of 0.3585, 0.3598, 0.3605, 0.3614, 0.3639, 0.3668, 0.37, 0.3734, 0.3738,
0.3773, and 0.385 MNm for 0.5, 1, 1.5, 2, 3, 4, 5, 6, 7, 8, and 9% increases respectively. This represents
a percentage increase of between 0.85 and 0.91% over the baseline maximum for percentage increase cases
between 0.5 and 9%. Hence these blade bending moments are considered negligible as the extreme load
represents a small percentage increase which would otherwise be experienced during turbulent operations.
5.3
FFR controller including pitch actuation capability
This chapter presents the results from the modiﬁed second parametric FFR controller which includes pitch
actuation to further improve the response of the WTG. This short presentation of results aims to prove that
the torque controlled FFR controller can be further enhance through use of pitch actuation. It also proves
that for failed cases, recovery is made possible with a careful choice of pitch variables. A quick summary
table is presented showing the input variables (explained in chapter 4.5) used for the controller per wind
speed for the cases of 5 and 9%. These variables were determined after approximately 5 iterations of testing
to tune the values and allow all cases to recover. Further testing should optimise and tailor these values to
a greater degree for both wind speed and percentage increases.

vation events
Figure 5.20: Loading response at 10 m/s for 5% increase, showing more unstable response due to pitch
The case above shows how with poor calibration of pitch values, an unstable response is produced and occurs
for the higher wind speed range seen at 10 m/s. This instability is the reason signiﬁcantly higher loads are
experienced at 10 m/s. Although the generator power complies with the ENTSO-E regulations, the RPM
swings below and over the RPM setpoint creating a potentially large positive feedback situation between the
generator torque and pitch activation. This response under more realistic application could be troublesome,
and future works to construct a more durable torque-pitch controlled FFR response should be explored.
Finally, the total recovery time for each case is seen to mostly improve. This is observed in the ﬁnal
columns of tables 14 and 15. Where it should be remembered that for wind speeds 7, 8 and 9 m/s, the
recovery was made possible with pitch and hence a overall improvement in recovery time is made. For the 5%
percent case; 3, 4 and 5 m/s showed a slight increase in recovery time of between 1 and 8 seconds. While for
the 9% case; 3 and 10 m/s showed a larger increase between 18 and 30 seconds. Again this can be improved
with more tailored or dynamic pitch variables which are able to closely track the most optimal operating
region of the WTG during FFR shown in ﬁgure 4.4. This should lead to a total reduction of recovery over
all wind speeds including those with a positive result in tables 14 and 15.
The issue with setting a constant pitch with respect to the RPM and the region (as is currently conducted
in this controller), is that it is not activated to optimise Cp. The pitch control should be a function of TSR
such that the most optimal coeﬃcient of power is achieved such that:
Cp,θt(λt) = θt+1
Where TSR is given by λ at time step t, and θt+1 is the most optimal pitch possible with respect to lowering
the Ct. This would act much like the torque controller below rated wind speed, where it tracks the MPPT
region seen in ﬁgure 2.3 through interpolation of a table of optimal values. This study has shown that pitch
activation is of beneﬁt to the FFR contribution from WTG’s. Hence future work should be dedicated to
building a table of optimal pitch values with respect to the TSR with the function to increase Cp while
also limiting Ct. This table could be called upon during FFR while below rated WS, and should reduce all
recovery times while hopefully remaining under the maximum loading conditions normally experienced by
the turbine.
Furthermore, a torque-pitch controlled FFR strategy much like that produced in this thesis should be
employed above rated wind. This should also be explored as the next step to ensure the WTG’s can contribute
FFR over all operational WS’s. This should be easily accomplished as the energy in the wind (hence the
available aerodynamic torque over the rotor) is much greater than the demanded generator power limited
by the rated capacity of the machine. Hence, overproduction should be sustained by pitching back toward
optimal and hence maintain the rotor speed at its rated value.
45
==PAGE:56==
Modelling & Control of Wind Turbines for Fast Frequency Support
Danmarks Tekniske Universitet
6
Implementation of FFR on a Wind Farm level
This chapter will discuss the overall ﬁndings made in chapter 5 and discuss proposed methodologies of
how exactly the FFR could be applied most optimally on a WF scale. Hence, the aim is to address the third
research question: How can WTG’s be optimised through better control and farm coordination? Chapter 3.4
stipulates that at a maximum, 50 MW of FFR provision is allowed to lie behind a single point of failure [18],
hence multiple turbines can collectively contribute to the FFR provision. This allows for the opportunity for
turbine coordination for better supply of FFR. The amount of turbines depends of the WF’s pre-qualiﬁed
capacity. Thus if each 15 MW turbine supports the grid for all wind speeds at an over production amount of
1% of its rated, then each turbine can supply 0.15 MW of provisional FFR. This would indicate a maximum
total of 333 turbines could bid while not exceeding the 50 MW limit. This size WF would represent the largest
non existent farm as the total capacity would be approximately 5 GW. Furthermore, an overproduction of
5% of the rated power, (i.e. 0.75 MW per turbine), would indicate that a total of approximately 66 15 MW
turbines could supply FFR provision without exceeding the limit of 50 MW. This results in a realistic 1
GW farm in which all turbines would have to supply the FFR provision to approach the maximum limit.
Therefore, an increase of over 5% would therefore potentially have to be capped to not exceed a maximum
50 MW of support.
This indicates that if the WF manager (now a Balancing Service Provider) bids for a 5% overproduction
per turbine, a realistic oﬀshore WF should be able to supply the FFR market using all turbines without any
concern of breaching the 50 MW. This is especially true considering that realistically there is a factor for
availability and downtime for individual turbines. As previously stated lower overproduction values cannot
reach the 50 MW limit unless the WF is a 5 GW capacity WF with only a single export cable (which is
extremely unlikely). Henceforth all existing oﬀshore farms should be able to participate at full capacity for
≤5% to maximise the FFR revenue, and coordination between x amount of turbines should be explored.
An important point should be discussed about the motivation for this coordination. Realistic application
of a WTG supplying a FFR market will have to function under the harsher varying environmental conditions
in which this thesis does not account for. The results from the low ﬁdelity simulations in chapter 5 have
proven that FFR capability from WTG’s are possible; but only if the available aerodynamic torque exceeds
the generator torque during the recovery region.
For some cases simulated in chapter 5, this diﬀerence
was minuscule and hence almost resulted in failure to recover. Therefore if simulated under more realistic
conditions, recovery may not be possible unless support and coordination from other turbines are used to
assist recovery for some struggling cases. This support could come in the form of a supporting turbine (called
the support turbine) helping to compensate power during the recovery of the primary turbine.
For this
strategy to occur, a coupling of 2 or more turbines may need to coordinate. This will therefore aﬀect the
amount of turbines in a given farm that can actively produce FFR power; hence also eﬀecting the amount
of power which could be bid for by the Balancing Service Provider. This concept of coupling was brieﬂy
described and presented schematically in ﬁgure 3.4, where below shows the primary turbine response.
Figure 6.1: Reduction of recovery time via a lowering of the primary WTG’s lower recovery limit (not to
scale)
46
==PAGE:57==
Modelling & Control of Wind Turbines for Fast Frequency Support
Danmarks Tekniske Universitet
The idea behind this coupling is that the lower limit of the primary turbine can be dropped from the ENTSO-
E regulated limit of 25% of the pre-qualiﬁed capacity (variable deﬁned as reclim in chapter 4.4), to any new
amount which can facilitate the recovery. The lowered limit decreases the demanded generator torque as
shown through the reduction of recovery power in ﬁgure 6.1. Where in this ﬁgure, the lower limit is dropped
to 100% as an example. This ﬁgure shows that during the recovery region, power generation under two
diﬀerent trapezoid paths are displayed (one green and one blue). The blue has an increased lower limit
of 100%, while the green has the 25% lower limit adhering to the ENTSO-E regulations. A reduction of
Trecovery for the blue trapezoid is also experienced as a result of a faster recovering rotor speed due to less
demanded opposing generator power. If the primary turbine is to recover under the blue trapezoid recovery
path, compensation of this extra exceeding 75% of allocated recovery from the supporting turbines must
occur. Hence, multiple supporting turbines will have to compensate during this recovery time through an
over production with duration exactly equal to the new recovery time of the primary turbine. This must
be achieved while also having the supporting turbines recoveries collectively not exceed the 25% lower limit
of the primary turbine.
This idea is presented in ﬁgure 6.2 below, where the blue curve represents the
overproduction for x amount of supporting turbines given by ki where i ≤x.
Figure 6.2: Superposition of primary FFR turbine with supporting turbines to assist recovery of primary
turbine (not to scale)
This compensation is shown in ﬁgure 6.2 above, and displays how the primary turbine in orange is able to
recover power faster due to a more natural re-balancing between the generator and aerodynamic torques
(explained in equation 5.1). The re-balancing is more uninhibited by allowing the generator torque to drop
lower to meet the available torque in the wind. Hence, the orange primary turbine recovers more successfully
but is directly compensated by the blue curve representing the overproduction from the supporting turbines.
These blue and orange curves superimpose into the dotted red line which represents the ﬁnal ENTSO-E
regulated FFR response from the multiple coordinated WTG’s.
However, support during this recovery
period from supporting turbines must overproduce synchronously to ensure the super position never exceeds
the 25% limit.
Following the recovery of the primary turbine, recovery of the supporting turbines must now begin but
with the 25% limit applied collectively between the x amount of turbines. The beneﬁt of allowing supporting
turbines to compensate is that these turbines function only in the region of recovery, and as stipulated by
the ENTSO-E regulations, the shape of recovery does not matter [18]. Furthermore, these support turbines
are not bound by the ENTSO-E regulations as long as the 25% lower limit of the primary turbines is not
exceeded. Henceforth they can overproduce in much the same way as in the ﬁrst parametric study. In other
words, the supporting turbines can overproduce for x amount of seconds and recover without any further
debilitating regulations including the ramp-up, ramp-down, and buﬀer regulations shown in ﬁgure 3.3. This
enables a saving of kinetic energy in the rotor, making them better able to support the primary turbine which
must adhere to all regions and conditions.
This saved kinetic energy could be crucial because as previously stated, this thesis has assumed simplistic
conditions which may unfavourably aﬀect the realistic ability to recover. The simulations are conducted
under a steady incoming wind with no turbulence, no wake eﬀects from neighbouring turbines, no tower
shadow and ﬁnally no wind shear. Therefore, although the results showed promising recoveries in chapter 5,
47
==PAGE:58==
Modelling & Control of Wind Turbines for Fast Frequency Support
Danmarks Tekniske Universitet
the ability to supply FFR consistently under more realistic conditions may require extra support to complete
the FFR cycle. Hence, this chapter conducted a small investigation about how the primary turbine can be
supported using secondary turbines through use of HAWC2.
The primary turbine selected is the torque controlled 5% increase case at 7 m/s. This particular case
showed a divergence of torques, hence recovery was not made possible, this is seen in ﬁgure 6.3a. A comparison
between the normal 25% lower limit case and the response from the 100% lower limit test case shows that
the recovery is then made possible, as shown in ﬁgure 6.3 below.

It can be seen in ﬁgure 6.3b that due to the lowering of the recovery lower limit, when the recovery period
begins at 420 seconds, the generator torque is lower than the available aerodynamic torque allowing the
RPM to increase with ease. While in ﬁgure 6.3a, when recovery begins at 420 seconds, the generator torque
is initially higher than the available aerodynamic torque creating the divergence seen between torques and
hence the resulting failure of the RPM recovery. Due to the lower recovery limit of the primary turbine, the
support turbines need to compensate for 75% (100 - 25%), this is therefore simulated. The support duration
for this case is very excessive and will result in 20 seconds of support. It is known from the ﬁrst parametric
study that this is a long support duration even for small demanded amounts of over production. Hence, the
risk is that the support WTG’s may require a recovery which would collectively exceed the total allowable
25%. To simulate the support turbines for diﬀerent cases, parameters are ﬁrst deﬁned as shown below with
respect to the primary turbines pre-qualiﬁed capacity given by a.

The results above show that even for a large number of supporting turbines, the torques still diverge and
result in a failure of recovery which would inevitably be seen past the 480 second duration of this simulation.
Decreasing the load on the support turbines via increasing the primary turbines lower limit to 80, 60 or even
50% would require less magnitude of power support; however the supporting turbines will have to support
for a greater duration. This as seen from the ﬁrst parametric study and the results above result in failure
of recovery. Therefore short overproduction duration’s are more optimal for WTG overproduction, and a
focus should be on decreasing the duration of overproduction. A solution could be to implement a step wise
recovery of the primary turbine for example stepping from 100 to 80, to 60% etc. lower where each step could
have a short duration in which the support turbines could easily recovery from (i.e. 5 seconds each step), or
lowering the limit further than 100%. Finally, the pitch torque controller could instead be implemented for
the supporting turbines providing more power and potentially the ability to recover.
The torque pitch controller is also tested on the support turbine FFR assistance with a reduction of
0.5°during the full duration of support while the RPM remains under the set point. The response is shown
in appendix in chapter 9.5 for a 1.5% increase in power for the primary turbine at 7 m/s, for 2 and 3
