The torque pitch support shown above in ﬁgures 6.9a and 6.9b show a successful assistance for both coupling
cases. Furthermore a step wise increase in the primary turbines lower limit or a further reduction past 100%
could also potentially lead to successful coupling. However, regardless of these successes, the ﬁndings heavily
suggest that this method is not recommended.
The exact recovery time of the primary turbine must be known prior to the supporting turbines activation,
and this is extremely variable and dependent on external environmental conditions.
These externalities
includes varying wind speed, turbulence, air density, the presence of rain, performance of the turbine (i.e.
state of the blades), etc., all of which are uncontrollable to the WF manager. The supporting turbines must
know the duration of support to the precise second to avoid a negative superposition of the recovery which
could exceed the 25% limit. This is an issue which can be observed in the appendix chapter 9.5 in ﬁgure
9.22; in which the support turbines were initially simulated for 26 seconds, where instead it should have
been 27.5 seconds. This lead to a large 1.5 second drop in recovery power which fell below the 25% lower
limit. Hence it is critical that the support turbines cannot begin recovery until the primary turbine has
returned to steady state operation. This condition if implemented into the supporting turbines controller
could lead to a domino eﬀect in which more supporting turbines must be employed to correct the failure of
the primary turbine and other supporting turbines. These extra supporting turbines could also fail under
the same pretext, and result in x amount of turbines unable to supply the market with any kind of power,
or the Balancing Service Provider being penalised heavily by the TSO for exceeding the lower limit.
This chapter has therefore indicated that the implementation of the FFR support from turbines should
happen simultaneously and collectively, and not with the recovery coordination strategy. The coordination
will also aﬀect the amount of turbines that can actively produce FFR power; which eﬀectively lowers the
amount of power which could be bid for by the Balancing Service Provider. The maximum limit of FFR
provision is 50 MW, which realistically cannot be exceeded with demands of ≤5% from every available 15
MW turbine. Hence all WTG’s should optimally act as the primary turbines to supply the FFR market,
and not the supporting which individually contribute no FFR capacity. Although coupling of turbines for
support is successful under the above simpliﬁed conditions, it realistically provides a larger risk of a domino
eﬀect in which more supporting WTG’s are drawn in to assist failing neighbouring support turbines.
51
==PAGE:62==
Modelling & Control of Wind Turbines for Fast Frequency Support
Danmarks Tekniske Universitet
7
Conclusion and future work
The objective of this work has been to investigate the capability of WTG’s supporting the electricity grid.
The WTG investigated is the latest Reference Wind Turbine from the IEA with a capacity of 15 MW, where
the support explored is a fast frequency response. This response contributes to the FFR market such that
an active power injection is inserted into the system when the frequency of the grid drops below a threshold.
For the Nordic region investigated, this is either 49.5, 49.6 or 49.7 Hz depending on the activation time of
0.7, 1, or 1.3 seconds respectively. The objective was approached in an iterative design strategy, in which
two parametric studies were conducted using HAWC2 Aeroelastic software. An expansion on the second
parametric study allowed for the introduction of pitch actuation during the FFR process. For each study,
a FFR controller was custom built to simulate the desired responses, these included two torque controllers,
and the extended torque-pitch controller. The ﬁrst parametric FFR controller activated an over production
for variable duration t, which allowed for a ramp up. The second parametric controller was designed around
the Nordic Inertia challenge, in which the technical requirements were used to characterise and build the
controller to adhere to ENTSO-E regulations. Finally, pitch was implemented to optimise the responses seen
from the second parametric controller study by exploiting the characteristics of the DTU basic controller.
Within each study, several scenarios were simulated and all results were recorded and analysed. A ﬁnal
analysis on the implementation of FFR on a WF scale was also explored using the models and results built
from the previous parametric studies. These investigations had the objective of answering three research
questions initially proposed, these include:
1. How can the turbine be controlled in order to meet speciﬁc regulations of a fast frequency market, such
as those speciﬁed by the Nordic Challenge to supply their FFR market?
2. How much overproduction is possible, and how can this support be supplied?
3. How can WTG’s be optimised through better control and farm coordination?
The ﬁrst research question is addressed by using the technical requirements of the ENTSO-E Nordic Iner-
tia Challenge to construct the various source codes which compile to create the .dll controller ﬁles. This
was implemented in the second parametric study, as well as the extended torque-pitch controlled FFR con-
troller. These second parametric FFR controllers incorporate both a torque and torque-pitch controlled FFR
response, and both successfully adhere to the full regulations stipulated by the Nordic conditions. These
regulations ensure a 1 second activation time, a 5 second over production duration, a 5 second ramp down, a
10 second buﬀer period, followed by a recovery region of duration dependent directly on the available aero-
dynamic torque remaining in the rotor. This was successfully implemented while also including a 25% lower
recovery limit, all of which indicated positive results showing that FFR is possible for modern day WTG’s.
However these controllers are tested under simpliﬁed wind conditions which don’t account for wind shear,
tower shadow, and turbulence. Furthermore, these controllers utilise simple IF statements to activate the
varying regions of the FFR response. This simplistic approach provided valuable data for the second and
third research questions, however will fail under more realistic simulations.
The second research question is explored through the analyses of the results gained from the numerous
case simulations of the ﬁrst and second parametric studies using the respective FFR controllers. The design
and build of the controllers were completed under an iterative approach, in which the ﬁrst study tested the
turbines capability to ramp up, overproduce and successfully recover. While the second study implemented
all other conditions to be met. The ﬁrst parametric study explored percentage increases between 4 and
11%. This resulted in a FFR energy injection of between 4.772 and 12.488 kWh; this amount depended
on the percentage increase, wind speed and the maximum overproduction time with respect to mechanical
and electrical limits of the machine. These results are tabulated and indicate that the highest energy yields
occur at the lower wind speeds between 3 and 4 m/s. The energy yield then signiﬁcantly decreases in the
mid range between 5 and 9 m/s, where 10 m/s shows an increase in energy output. This is accounted for
and corresponds to the regular turbine controllers three operating conditions before reaching rated power.
Finally, a peak energy output was also determined for the 9% increase in power case. The energy output
intuitively increases as the percentage increase becomes larger, however this trend begins to dip after 9% due
to excessive loading which limits the maximum allowable duration of overproduction.
The ﬁrst parametric study does not adhere to the ENTSO-E regulations, but is used to test the capability
of the WTG and the ability to overproduce and recover. Furthermore, the results indicated that the 5 second
support duration was the most appropriate for the second parametric simulations; given the WTG’s rotor
failed to recover for high overproduction duration’s. This is further reinforced, as the mechanical and electri-
cal limits were often met or exceeded during the long duration simulations conducted in the ﬁrst parametric
study.
52
==PAGE:63==
Modelling & Control of Wind Turbines for Fast Frequency Support
Danmarks Tekniske Universitet
The second parametric and additional pitch actuation studies, both address the ability of the WTG to
supply the ENTSO-E regulated FFR cycle with respect to the loading on critical components. The torque
controlled FFR controller is tested for cases ranging from 0.5 to 9% over all wind speeds below rated wind.
Only 0.5 and 1% increase cases allowed for the full FFR cycle to complete and recover over all wind speeds.
Failure of recovery is primarily experienced within wind speeds including 7, 8 and 9 m/s, hence careful ap-
plication of torque controlled FFR should be applied within this region.
The loading from all torque controlled simulated cases are compared with the maximum loading occurring
during the baseline operations. This baseline is simulated over a wider wind speed range from 3 to 20 m/s to
cover the full loading capability of the structure, and hence to determine the true maximum loading it can
withstand. The results were extremely positive in which loads including the blade thrust, tower root bending
moment, blade root bending moment and the shaft bearing bending moment never exceeded the maximum
baseline conditions during 0.5 to 9% FFR increase cases. This was with exception of each 10 m/s case, where
the blade root bending moment was exceeded by between 0.85 and 0.91%, representing a considerably small
0.36 and 0.39 MNm increase respectively. Hence, this is considered negligible and indicates that no signiﬁcant
extreme loading occurs as a result of FFR for wind speeds ≤10 m/s.
The torque-pitch controller was then tested with the primary intention to recover all previously failed
cases. The cases simulated included 5 and 9% FFR increases, and are explored over all wind speeds from
3 to 10 m/s. The implementation of pitch managed to allow for a higher deliverable energy, and hence the
full recovery of the Nordic FFR cycle was made possible over all tested wind speeds. This was only achieved
by the torque controller for up to a 1% FFR increase. Hence, this full recovery over all tested wind speeds
indicates that all increases ≤9% are achievable with the torque-pitch controller. It is therefore recommended
that the overproduction be supplied with a torque-pitch controller; as higher amounts of FFR provision can
be bid for over a wider range of wind speeds by the Balancing Service Provider.
Finally, the last research question hopes to address how the WTG’s (which employ the second parametric
controllers) be better optimised through collective activation and farm coordination to supply the FFR pro-
vision. Up to 50 MW of FFR is allowed to be supplied behind one point of failure. This indicated that for
today’s largest oﬀshore WF, a percentage increase of ≤5% will never exceed this limit, while higher percent-
ages may have to limit the amount of supplying turbines. Hence future oﬀshore farm managers can act as
the Balancing Service Providers and can utilise every available WTG for bidding for less than a 5% increase.
While for ≥5% increase within large oﬀshore WF’s, some available turbines would have to be limited, hence
opening the opportunity for the remaining WTG’s to supply coordinated support. If all pre-qualiﬁed WTG’s
activate at the same time using the same individual FFR controller per turbine, the responses will superim-
pose to collectively supply the Nordic regulated FFR successfully. Recovery per turbine may vary depending
on its own performance ability, however each turbine will not exceed its on 25% lower recovery limit, hence
collectively the farms lower recovery limit will also not be exceeded.
Coordination between turbines to assist recovery was also explored and a successful case was demon-
strated. However this method is not recommended and should be avoided because each turbine has a varying
capability which develops over its useful lifetime. Hence the exact recovery time per turbine cannot be know
prior to a supporting turbines activation of power assistance. Any mismatch of duration between the primary
and supporting turbine during recovery of the former will result in exceedance of the collective lower limit,
and penalties from the TSO will occur. Furthermore, if supporting turbines fail to support due to excessive
demand from the primary turbine, a domino eﬀect in which more supporting turbines are forced into assis-
tance may occur, and lead to more supporting turbines failing and hence supplying no energy at all.
Finally, results from the thesis indicate that the use of WTG’s over other competing suppliers may be
more suitable for the FFR market. The advantage WTG’s have is that an activation time of 0.7 seconds or
less could be registered for. Hence, the WF Balancing Service Providers have the opportunity to leverage
the ability of the turbine to overproduce more rapidly. Henceforth, the WF managers could lobby for shorter
activation times to make WF’s more competitive and potentially enable the gain of a larger market share.
Hence the most optimal application of the FFR economically could actually be with a 0.7 second activation
time or less.
7.1
Future work
This thesis was successful in proving that WTG’s can provide ENTSO-E regulated FFR support through
use of the custom built controllers. However, many improvements can be made to these dynamic ﬁles which
will further strengthen the controllers ability and applicability to realistic WF’s. The controllers currently
utilise simple IF statements to activate the desired conditions at speciﬁc times; this simplistic approach would
fail under more realistic simulations. Future work should incorporate a better feedback controller which will
allow for the desired output currently provided by the controllers, while also coping with the varying wind
53
==PAGE:64==
Modelling & Control of Wind Turbines for Fast Frequency Support
Danmarks Tekniske Universitet
conditions and structural responses under loading. Hence, following this update, the testing of the controller
while allowing for wind shear, tower shadow and ultimately turbulence will yield ﬁnal and more conclusive
results regarding the research questions.
Development of the controllers should also focus on a lower activation time.
Future work should re-
simulate with activation option 1, which is 0.7 seconds activated at 49.5 Hz. This would reduce the overall
cycle time which is a crucial factor to a safer recovery as the studies found that extra time (0.3 seconds)
between activation and the end of the buﬀer zone puts more strain on the rotor’s ability to re-generate
speed. Furthermore, option 1 will also activate less frequently due to the lower detonation limit of 49.5
Hz. Less activation will have a reduced impact on the fatigue loading on the turbine which would otherwise
accumulate from continuous implementation of FFR. Finally, simulations showed that a high activation rate
doesn’t provide any issue for the WTG, except for a small overshoot for which all fell below the upper limit
of 35% of the pre-qualiﬁed capacity. Hence future work should also be dedicated to exploring even faster
activation rates of less than 0.7 seconds, or even step up activation’s. This will allow the Balancing Service
Provider to decide which activation frequency to bid on at their discretion, and have the ability to use this
property as a leverage tool to out bid any other competing generators due to a faster activation capability.
Future work on the torque controller should include the implementation of a high rate ramp-down between
the buﬀer and recovery regions. This will unfortunately put slightly more pressure on the recovery process
due to extra imposed time, however it is crucial as an undershoot occurs for every simulated case conducted
in this thesis. The small undershoot during this step down exceeded the lower limit for a duration exceeding
no longer than 2 tenths of a second for every simulated case. Although small, future work should simply
avoid this step down to recovery, by including an extremely fast ramp-down instead.
Finally, the torque controller response had another issue in which future work should address. The power
following the recovery zone increases drastically leading to an overshoot as seen in ﬁgure 5.13b. This is
because the rotor speed begins to increase rapidly, while the torque is held at a decreasing rate to maintain
the condition of the 25% lower limit. This issue can be further improved with a better controller strategy
which doesn’t hold the generator torque such that the lower limit is matched; but instead works such that
the power is limited by 25% but gradually increases back to the setpoint value as the rotor speed increases.
The torque-pitch controller also requires signiﬁcant future work, as the current controller has simply
proven recovery is possible over all wind speeds through the joint employment of pitch and torque control.
Improvements should be dedicated to the functionality of the controller, as it is currently very primitive and
requires many setpoint inputs with respect to the tested wind speed. These inputs require further calibration,
and it would be more optimal if a reference table of optimal pitch values were called on as a function of TSR
during FFR. Furthermore, the torque-pitch controller should be developed to work together more optimally,
as realistic operations such as those including turbulence and other wind phenomena could create serious
conﬂicts. This is because the torque and pitch responses will interfere with each other creating instability.
Hence more detailed studies should explore this controller as its performance is promising.
Finally, future work concerning FFR provision on a WF scale should focus more on individual WTG’s
and their ability to supply this active power ancillary service using the torque-pitch control below rated
wind. This will allow for all WTG’s to independently provide FFR without the requirement of coordinated
supporting turbines. This on a farm scale collectively amounts to a larger additional support for the grid,
as most realistic wind farms are not large enough to breach the 50 MW limit. This therefore allows for
the permission of all turbines to contribute FFR, hence resulting in the highest revenue. If turbines are
instead utilised for coordinated support, these supporting WTG’s will not contribute any additional FFR,
and therefore an opportunity cost is incurred.
If the controller is updated as stipulated above, and is tested with wind shear, tower shadow and turbulence
and fails to recover, then coordination strategy is the only option.
Future work can explore a further
facilitation by lowering the primary turbines recovery limit further. This thesis only tested for a maximum
lower limit of 100%, and had resulted in excessive recovery times for which the supporting turbines had to
overproduce for 20 seconds and more. If however the limit was lower (i.e. 150, or 200%), then the recovery time
would reduce to a more realistic support duration for supporting turbines. Therefore allowing the support
turbines to easily assist through a shorter overproduction duration. Furthermore, if the support turbines
were to produce the estimated shortened duration plus a contingency of x seconds, then the risk of breaching
the collective lower limit of 25% through a mismatch of recovery and support times is diminished. This will
allow for safer coordination and provide FFR cycles that are robust and applicable for grid stabilisation.
Finally, future work should incorporate the torque-pitch activation during FFR control above rated wind
speeds. This should be easily included as the available energy in the wind should exceed that utilised by
the generator during normal production above rated. Hence, when FFR activates the TSR will reduce, but
pitching toward optimal will increase power and sustain the over production.

9.8
.htc FFR controller implementation
The following sub-chapters are the relevant sections of the .htc for the implementation of each three controllers.
Where the ... indicates the insertion points and begins at line 522 of the baseline .htc, where the full .htc can
